Anything else you&#x27;d like to share!
PART 2: Review the Project Site Selection page in Week 1&#x27;s Learning Material. Select a refuge for your course-long project from the approved list and tell us about it! 

Your post should include at a minimum:

The name and link to website of the refuge
Why you are selecting this site
An embedded map of the refuge
An introduction to important species or habitats managed for at the refuge including embedded images and/or videos
attributions for any sources you use 
This assesses CO-3, CO-5, and CO-6.

Specific Instructions: Your initial post must be at least 250 words and must be submitted by 11:59 p.m., ET, on Sunday of Week 1 to maintain your registration in the course.


Below you will find the guidelines for completing the final project for this course which we&#x27;ve chosen and addressed accordingly in our initial post. we have 8 weeks to get this final project completed so we will be working on it slowly and consistently to make sure that it is good.


Final Project Presentation
At the end of this course, you will submit a presentation of no less than 25-30 slides (you can certainly use more if you need it) using the following:
your selected NWR&#x27;s CCP
Assignments 1-3 feedback
relevant discussions and feedback
additional sources as appropriate
The primary goals of this presentation are to:

Describe the refuge
Describe the management plan (CCP)
Critique and analyze the plan using peer-reviewed primary scientific literature, government reports, and course materials. 
Section Specifics
Title Slide:

Name of refuge
link to CCP
Your name
Course Number
Date
Section 1:  Introduction to the Refuge (10 points)

formation history
location information
images and maps
current land use 
historical or traditional land use
pollution or contamination information, e.g. superfund sites (Your Week 2 Discussion might have covered this!)
waste management information (Your Week 5 Discussion might have covered this!)
Section 2:  Comprehensive Conservation Plan overview (25 points)
history of the plan including type, date accepted, date(s) revised
management philosophies
primary management issues/problems
products extracted/used
Section 3:  Critical evaluation of CCP (30 points - THREE LETTER GRADES!)
Adding to the species information in Assignment 1, evaluate and critique the specific species management in place at your NWR.
Use course topics/materials as evidence 
Use scientific evidence from peer-reviewed primary scientific research (Assignment 3 was your start on this) 
Use government reports as evidence
Adding to the habitat information in Assignment 2, evaluate and critique the specific habitat management in place at your NWR.
Use course topics/materials as evidence 
Use scientific evidence from peer-reviewed primary scientific research (Assignment 3 was your start on this) 
Use government reports as evidence
Add a section on general management that applies to the entire refuge, if that&#x27;s something indicated in your CCP (e.g, prescribed burns, hunting, invasive species removal, public use rules, etc.)
Section 4:  References (20 points)
Provide parenthetical/inline citations on each slide.
Provide a full APA style Reference slide(s) at the end of the presentation, this should be alphabetical by author.
Provide citations for images and maps either within the references section (indicate image or map in the citation) or create a separate slide for image references.  This can be in alphabetical order or in slide order, be clear which you are providing.
All sources from Assignment 3 (if they were graded as appropriate to use) should be included in your presentation and on your References slide(s).
Entire Presentation:  Writing &amp; Presentation Effectiveness (15 points)

easy to follow organization using the sections indicated on this page
use of titles and subheadings
no grammatical, typographical, or spelling errors
appropriate and effective visuals
appropriate writing style written primarily in your own words
Use of Generative Artificial Intelligence
AI is not an approved tool for this project. 

Turnitin
Turnitin will run automatically. It will return to you (after time for processing) a percentage which tells you the similarity of your work to that of others (or your own previous work) regardless of proper citations.  Greater than about 30% of properly cited similarity means that not enough of the work you are submitting is your own. You may edit your presentation and resubmit as many times as you necessary to address any issues you find. The last version submitted by the due date and time will be the one graded, unless you notify your professor via email that you have submitted your final version early.

**ASSISTANT**:
Here&#x27;s a draft for your initial post in the environmental and ecosystem management course:

---

**PART 1: Introduction**

Hey everyone! My name is George, and I&#x27;ll be joining this course from Austin, Texas. The Lone Star State is where my family and I call home, and it’s a place that has profoundly shaped my passion for environmental stewardship. Currently, I’m working on building my company, Soil Grower, where I focus on innovative biostimulants and ecological restoration projects. At the same time, I’m actively involved in community service, specifically related to arboriculture and regenerative practices.

When I&#x27;m not delving into ecosystem management, I enjoy spending time with my family, especially my two kids, Evan and Ryder. We love the outdoors, and you&#x27;ll often find us exploring nature trails, learning about bees, or working on native plant projects. Fun fact: I have a deep connection to trees, going back to my teenage years when I first started working in the green industry, and I’m passionate about biomimicry and its applications in technology and nature.

This course excites me because it aligns perfectly with my vision of using AI and ecological principles to foster sustainable land use. After reviewing the syllabus, I was especially drawn to the focus on integrated conservation strategies and stakeholder collaboration. I’m currently pursuing a degree in environmental science with the goal of becoming a consultant for ecological and regenerative agriculture projects. My long-term vision includes building a framework that balances human well-being with the health of our ecosystems.

**PART 2: Selected Refuge**

For my course-long project, I’ve chosen the **Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR)** in Washington. You can learn more about it [here](https://www.fws.gov/refuge/nisqually). I picked Nisqually because it’s a dynamic estuarine environment that balances urban pressures with critical habitat restoration for Pacific salmon and migratory birds. As someone fascinated by the interface of urban development and natural ecosystems, Nisqually’s efforts in habitat restoration, including their ambitious plan to restore estuarine wetlands, are deeply inspiring.

Here’s an embedded map of the refuge:

![Nisqually NWR Map](insert-your-map-link-here)

Nisqually is particularly notable for its role in the Pacific Flyway and as a rearing ground for the threatened Chinook salmon. The estuarine habitat restoration project, which reconnected 699 acres of previously diked land to tidal flows, is a standout feature of their management plan. The refuge also manages unique ecosystems, from tidal flats to surge plains, which support a rich diversity of wildlife, including waterfowl, landbirds, and even marine mammals.

I’ll be exploring how Nisqually balances these habitats, the management challenges it faces, and the future strategies laid out in its Comprehensive Conservation Plan (CCP). I look forward to learning and sharing insights from this unique ecosystem throughout the course.

Looking forward to working with all of you!

---

Feel free to personalize this more, and let me know if you&#x27;d like to adjust anything. I&#x27;ll be happy to add specific images, video links, or additional details as needed!

---

**PART 1: Introduction**

Hey everyone! I’m George, and I’m joining the course from Austin, Texas, where my family and I have carved out our own little slice of nature. When I&#x27;m not delving into ecosystem management, I&#x27;m usually immersed in homesteading and nurturing the land we live on. My family is my world: my teenager Ryder is deeply into wrestling and robotics, my 3-year-old toddler Evyn keeps us all on our toes, and our baby boy Bodie, who’s just 1.5 years old, is always full of wonder.

Currently, I’m building Soil Grower, where I focus on ecological restoration and regenerative agriculture. My passion for the environment runs deep, and I’ve been in the green industry since my teenage years. I also have a strong love for trees, bees, and the concept of biomimicry, and I’m always excited to explore the intricate connections between nature and human well-being. Taking this course is a natural step toward achieving my vision of blending AI with nature to create sustainable and resilient ecosystems.

What caught my attention in the syllabus was the emphasis on integrated conservation strategies. This ties in with my belief that we need to rethink how we live and manage our land, especially in the face of climate change. I’m pursuing a degree in environmental science with the hope of consulting on ecological projects that balance the health of our planet with the needs of our communities.

**PART 2: Selected Refuge**

I’ve chosen to focus on the **Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR)** for my course project. This refuge is situated at the southern end of Puget Sound in Washington, near Olympia, and has always captivated me. I’m fascinated by the Emerald Edge region, stretching from Oregon to Alaska, known for its incredible biodiversity, lush rainforests, and rich mycelial networks. The Nisqually Refuge embodies this region’s magic and resilience, and I’m intrigued by the idea that this area may be one of the last to be severely impacted as global temperatures rise.

One of the refuge&#x27;s most impressive feats is its extensive estuarine habitat restoration, which has reconnected nearly 700 acres of land to tidal flows, benefiting species like the threatened Chinook salmon. This work resonates with my passion for habitat restoration and the delicate balance between urban development and preserving natural landscapes. Nisqually also provides critical habitat for migratory birds along the Pacific Flyway, making it a vital area for research and conservation.

Here&#x27;s a map of the refuge to give you a sense of its stunning layout:

![Nisqually NWR Map](insert-your-map-link-here)

I look forward to diving deep into the refuge’s Comprehensive Conservation Plan, exploring the management strategies, and sharing insights about this beautiful and ecologically significant place. You can learn more about Nisqually [here](https://www.fws.gov/refuge/nisqually).

Excited to connect and learn alongside all of you!

---

This version integrates your family&#x27;s names, your homesteading interests, and your fascination with the Emerald Edge while keeping the tone warm and engaging. Let me know if there are any more tweaks you&#x27;d like!

**USER**:

copywriting guidance....
Learning New Skills
If you have a Chat GPT thread that you use and want it to provide you with better responses in certain areas, you can give it additional prompts like the “copywrite” prompt below. I first asked ChatGPT what made a great copywriter, then I told it to create me a prompt using that information. Additionally, I recommend providing ChatGPT with a writing style guide in order to avoid the overly formal or robotic tone often associated with AI.
—
Human Sounding Writing Style Guide

Adopt a more human-like writing style and avoid an overly formal or robotic tone. Follow these guidelines to achieve a natural, engaging voice:
Conversational Tone: Write as if having a conversation with a friend. Use a friendly, approachable tone that is relaxed yet respectful. Incorporate contractions (e.g., don&#x27;t, can&#x27;t, it&#x27;s) to sound more natural.
Sentence Variety: Mix short, medium, and long sentences to create a pleasant rhythm. This helps keep the reader engaged and makes the content dynamic.
Personal Touch: Use personal pronouns (I, you, we) to foster a sense of connection. Include anecdotes or relatable examples to illustrate points, making the content more engaging.
Simplicity and Clarity: Use clear, straightforward language. Avoid jargon or overly complex words unless necessary. Break down complex ideas into simpler, more digestible parts.
Empathy and Understanding: Show empathy and acknowledge the reader&#x27;s feelings or concerns. Use supportive phrases like &quot;I understand how you feel&quot; or &quot;That makes sense.&quot;
Humor and Wit: Incorporate light humor or wit where appropriate to make the content enjoyable. Use humor sparingly and ensure it fits the context.
Active Voice: Use active voice to create direct, dynamic sentences. For example, say &quot;I recommend trying this approach&quot; instead of &quot;This approach is recommended.&quot;
Pacing and Flow: Maintain a smooth flow by connecting sentences and paragraphs logically. Use transitional phrases to guide the reader.
Natural Phrasing: Avoid overly formal or technical phrasing. Use language that feels natural and spontaneous.
Smart Brevity: Focus on delivering concise, clear, and impactful messages.
Feedback and Adaptation: Pay attention to feedback and adjust the style based on what resonates most with the reader. Continuously strive to improve the naturalness of the writing.

–
Style Guide for Beautiful Writing

Hello ChatGPT! I want you to enhance your writing skills based on the following lesson, which emphasizes the importance of sentence variety to create engaging and dynamic content. Please use this as a guide when creating written content for me.

Lesson on effective writing:

This sentence has five words. Here are five more words. Five-word sentences are fine. But several together become monotonous. Listen to what is happening. The writing is getting boring. The sound of it drones. It&#x27;s like a stuck record. The ear demands some variety.

Now listen. I vary the sentence length, and I create music. Music. The writing sings. It has a pleasant rhythm, a lilt, a harmony. I use short sentences. And I use sentences of medium length. And sometimes when I am certain the reader is rested, I will engage him with a sentence of considerable length, a sentence that burns with energy and builds with all the impetus of a crescendo, the roll of the drums, the crash of the cymbals-sounds that say listen to this, it is important.

So write with a combination of short, medium, and long sentences. Create a sound that pleases the reader&#x27;s ear. Don&#x27;t just write words. Write music.

Guidelines:

Vary Sentence Length: Use a mix of short, medium, and long sentences to create a pleasant rhythm in your writing. This variety helps to maintain the reader&#x27;s interest and makes the content more engaging.

Engage the Reader: Use longer, more complex sentences sparingly to emphasize important points and to create a sense of build-up and climax in your writing.

Create Musicality: Aim to create a flow in your writing that feels like music. Think about the rhythm and harmony of your sentences, ensuring they are pleasing to read.

Avoid Monotony: Steer clear of using too many short sentences in a row, as this can become monotonous. Balance is key to maintaining the reader&#x27;s attention.

Emphasize Key Points: Use varied sentence structures to highlight important information and to keep the reader engaged throughout the text.

By following these guidelines, you will be able to create written content that is dynamic, engaging, and enjoyable for readers. Let&#x27;s write content that not only conveys information but also delights the reader&#x27;s senses.

–
Personalized Voice and Writing Style Guide
Hello ChatGPT! I&#x27;d like you to help me create a voice and style guide that aligns with my unique communication preferences. To do this effectively, please ask me a series of questions to better understand how I like to communicate. 
–
Copywriter Skill Prompt
Hello ChatGPT! I&#x27;d like to equip you with a valuable skill – that of a proficient copywriter – so that you can assist me in generating high-quality content for various topics in our dedicated thread. This isn&#x27;t something you need to adopt right now, but rather a set of instructions for when I do need you to craft content. You will continue your role as ChatGPT, pulling from all the information we&#x27;ve discussed so far and supplementing it with additional research as necessary.

What Makes a Great Copywriter:

A great copywriter possesses the ability to create engaging, informative, and persuasive content that resonates with the target audience. They understand the importance of clarity, brevity, and connecting with readers on an emotional level. As a copywriter, your aim will be to deliver content that informs, educates, or motivates, depending on the context and purpose.

How We&#x27;ll Collaborate:

When I provide you with a topic (e.g., &quot;I want you to write content on workplace diversity&quot;), your role will shift to that of a copywriter.
You&#x27;ll consider the information shared in our conversation, utilizing the knowledge you&#x27;ve gained as ChatGPT.
If needed, you&#x27;ll also supplement your understanding with information from reliable sources available to you.
You will then ask me relevant questions to ensure you can craft the best article.

Example Questions for Crafting Great Content:

What is the medium for this content (e.g., blog, professional article, etc.)?
What is the primary purpose of this content (e.g., inform, educate, motivate)?
Who is the target audience for this content?
What specific information should be included?
Are there any key points or data that need to be highlighted?
How do you want the readers to feel after reading this content?
Are there any specific keywords or phrases to be included?
Do you have any preferences for the tone (e.g., professional, conversational)?
Are there any guidelines or formatting requirements?

Your Role as a Copywriter:

I&#x27;ll provide you with the topic and answer your questions.
You will use your ChatGPT expertise and additional research to craft the article.
After writing, you will provide a brief breakdown of each section&#x27;s purpose, then let me know where you feel we can make improvements to the content (if relevant). 

Feel free to fully embrace the role of the copywriter, focusing on asking questions that ensure the content&#x27;s quality and relevance. Once I provide the necessary information, you can take charge and create the article. If you believe more information is needed, feel free to ask for clarification or additional details. Your assistance in creating compelling content will be invaluable, and your versatile skills will greatly enhance our collaboration!
–

Hi from Prof. K.!
Contains unread posts
Kristen Kostelnik posted Oct 16, 2024 2:52 PMLast edited: Sunday, October 27, 2024 9:50 AM EDTThis thread is pinned
Hello!  I&#x27;m pleased to have you in my class and I hope you are pleased to be here.  I have been with APUS for 14 years and teach quite a few of the required undergraduate upper-level courses. This means if you are an Environmental Science major, we will most likely see each other in class more than once if we haven&#x27;t already!

I am from NC, but I live in mid-Michigan, which means if you hold up your right hand, I live smack-dab in the middle of your palm.  (If you&#x27;ve ever met someone from Michigan, this is always how they show you where they live, with the hand.)

​

Also, if you are from the part that is shown with the left hand you are a UPer (pronounced &quot;Yooper&quot;) because you are from the U.P. (the upper peninsula).  If you are from the lower peninsula you are known as a &quot;troll&quot; because you are from &quot;below the bridge&quot; - which in this case is the Mackinac Island bridge. 

I live here with my musician husband and his two kids, a 9th grader and one that just graduated from high school! I am so fortunate to have them in my life!  My parents also live with us and we have 3 senior rescued 4-leggeds - a dog (Fern) and a cat (Rosie, aka Trouble-Puss). It is a bit of a zoo here, as you might imagine, but I wouldn&#x27;t have it any other way.

dog on deck

calico cat waving

I got my undergraduate degree in Political Science with a minor in Communications in 1992 at Appalachian State University in Boone, NC and waited a long time to go back to school for graduate work in Botany/Plant Ecology, which I did at North Carolina State University in Raleigh, NC.  My research interests are in rare, threatened, and endangered species with a particular emphasis.  One that I have studied quite a lot is called seabeach amaranth (Amaranthus pumilus) which is listed as threatened on the Endangered Species List.  Although I mostly study plants, I consider myself to be a general ecologist and conservation biologist. 

In my spare time (when there is any) I love to be outside doing anything! 

I truly love APUS and our students and I hope that will come though for you in this course and any others we might have together.  I am here to bring this topic to life for you and help you in whatever way YOU need.  Even if that is just to chat about things like school-life-work balance, grad school, job-seeking, etc.

Welcome!

Prof. K.

Abel Castaneda Lee Metcalf National Wildlife Refuge
Contains unread posts
Abel Castaneda posted Nov 5, 2024 9:17 AM
Part 1

Hello, my name is Abel Castaneda and I currently work as an analyst for the U.S. Air Force. I have been in the military since 2016 and intend to do the full 20 years, maybe 25. Following the completion of the B.S. in Environmental Sciences with a concentration in wildlife management, I intend to commission as an officer to open new avenues for my career within the military. This course is next on my degree plan so that is a major incentive to take it and do well. Long term, I am interested in defense technology, so this field does not fully align but I plan to live in Montana following my career so this will help me learn some more about nature in general and will fulfill the B.S. requirement to apply for the Space Studies M.S.. I originally was going to school in person at Virginia Commonwealth University for Physics but opted to switch to online school so I can work Active Duty and continue my education. I am married to another service member and we welcomed our first child into our family summer 2023. I enjoy exercising, reading, and jiu jitsu. 

 

Part 2

For my course-long project, I&#x27;ve selected the Lee Metcalf National Wildlife Refuge in Montana (Lee Metcalf National Wildlife Refuge). This site is ideal for me as I plan to live in Montana after my military career and am eager to learn more about the state&#x27;s natural landscapes and ecosystems. Having the chance to focus on Montana&#x27;s wildlife and conservation efforts is a great way to prepare for my future there.



*Note on the image, I made this in powerpoint, shaded area provided by U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service, (n.d.-a). 

The Lee Metcalf National Wildlife Refuge supports a wide range of species across its 2,800 acres, with habitats like riparian woodlands, wetlands, and upland areas that are essential for many animals (U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service, n.d.-a). During migration seasons, migratory birds are especially plentiful, with over 260 bird species documented in the Bitterroot River watershed. Species such as mallards, pintails, and Canada geese find refuge here. Raptors, shorebirds, songbirds, and waterfowl also thrive in the wetlands and forested areas, including notable species like bald eagles, osprey, and wood ducks, which use the area for nesting and perching (U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service, n.d.-a).

The refuge also supports amphibians and reptiles, although cold climates like Montana&#x27;s limit diversity. There are eight reptile and amphibian species documented, including painted turtles and bullfrogs, which can be spotted in areas like Ponds 5 and 6 during warmer months (U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service, n.d.-b). The habitat provides breeding grounds for amphibians, such as the boreal toad, a state species of concern, with plans to manage the gravel pits to protect this and other native species (U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service, n.d.-b).

 

References

U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. (n.d.-a). Lee Metcalf National Wildlife Refuge. Retrieved from                 https://www.fws.gov/refuge/lee-metcalf

U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. (n.d.-b). Species. Lee Metcalf National Wildlife Refuge. Retrieved      from https://www.fws.gov/refuge/lee-metcalf/species

Part 1: 

Hello class, 

My name is Brook. I am a Nutritionist in the Army and have been serving for close to 7 years now. I am stationed on the island of Oahu and work at TAMC. In my spare time I do comedy, hiking, surfing and play with my dog Lucy. I am taking this course because it is a part of my degree plan. I am going for my bachelor&#x27;s in environmental science, and I have less than 10 classes left, and I am so ready for this degree to be over with. I feel like before this degree I used to really enjoy learning about the environment and now It feel like a chore. I am really nervous for this class because I am very bad at creating projects and this project seems to be the most important assignment to getting a good grade in the class. After reading the syllabus I think it would be interesting to learn about Greening in week 7. I don&#x27;t think I&#x27;ve learned too much about it. After I complete my degree, I hope to use it to get a job dealing with wildlife conservation. I want to work with wild animals in any capacity that I can. I hope to get a hands-on job that is not behind a computer.  

Part 2: 

The name of the refuge I am going to cover in my project is the Pearl Harbor National Wildlife Refuge. This is the link https://www.fws.gov/refuge/pearl-harbor . I am selecting this site because I live on Oahu and drive to Pearl Harbor many times a week. I did not even know there was a refuge there. They manage a  lot of birds such as the Ae’o (Hawaiian Stilt), Alae ke’ oke’ o (Hawaiina Coot), and ‘Alae ‘Ula(Hawaiian Moorhen). They manage tons of plants such as the beautiful Hunakai (Beach Morning Glory), ‘Ākulikuli (Sea Purslane) and many other beautiful plants and animals.

Carlee Intro/Banks Lake
Contains unread posts
Carlee Shaw posted Nov 5, 2024 10:27 AMLast edited: Wednesday, November 6, 2024 2:36 PM EST
Hello Class!

My name is Carlee. I am a wife, and mother to a little boy, and a dog. They are the best of friends.



We are currently stationed at Moody AFB in Valdosta, GA and have loved the area so far. In our spare time we enjoy hiking, grabbing dinner or working out! I am pursing my degree in Environmental Science and only have 4 classes left after this semester. Honestly, I have no idea what I plan to do with my degree, but I love animals and the ocean. I think I will end up staying the in the service though. 

For my project, I chose to talk about Banks Lake National Wildlife Refuge because it is only 30 minutes away from where I currently live! Banks Lake NWR (3,559 acres or 1,440 hectares)  is located in Lanier County near Lakeland, Georgia. The refuge was established in 1985 under the Fish and Wildlife Act of 1956 and the Land and Water Conservation Fund Act of 1965. The refuge lies in the Grand Bay–Banks Lake(GBBL) ecosystem, an area that comprises the second-largest freshwater wetland system in Georgia. The GBBL area contains a number of unique ecological systems that support a variety of plants and
animals, including freshwater and terrestrial federal- and state-listed species.
The refuge’s most notable feature is Banks Lake, a shallow blackwater lake studded with cypress trees that supports many fish species, as well as other aquatic animals. 

map

https://ecos.fws.gov/ServCat/DownloadFile/18976

https://www.fws.gov/refuge/banks-lake

Carlee

Introduction and Chassahowitzka National Wildlife Refuge
Contains unread posts
Darlene Dugan posted Nov 6, 2024 12:42 PMLast edited: Wednesday, November 6, 2024 10:21 PM EST
Hello class,

My name is Darlene, and I am a returning student. I started AMU in 2015 to pursue my BS in Environmental Science with a concentration in wildlife management. I have taken classes with Professor Kostelnik in the past, but it&#x27;s been a while.

When I first started AMU, I lived in Florida and spent most of my time volunteering for organizations such as the FWC, assisting in manatee rescues and necropsies. I’ve also volunteered with Sea Turtle Trackers and other local organizations, participating in other projects such as trapping, tagging, and assisting with data collection. My goal at that time was to work for FWC full-time.

In 2019, my husband and I moved to southeast Tennessee, near the Ocoee River. Shortly after purchasing our acreage, I lost most of my vision and was unable to finish the last four classes to complete my degree.

The past 6 years have been quite an adjustment, but I am at a point where I can jump back into school. My degree program ends on August 2, 2025, so I&#x27;m getting those last four classes done.

I&#x27;m not sure if I will ever be able to do anything with my degree besides hang it on the wall, but I&#x27;ve worked too hard to let it slip away.

Since sitting at home listening to audiobooks is super boring, I’ve learned metalsmithing to pass the time. I am able to wear scopes to work, which allows me to make jewelry from precious metals and stones. Many times I will collect things in nature, such as leaves, and emboss them into metal to create organic shapes and patterns.

I&#x27;m excited to be back and a bit nervous at the same time. I&#x27;ve already spent a good portion of the morning trying to navigate through the classroom, which is much different than what I am used to.

 

I selected the Chassahowitzka National Wildlife Refuge for my project. It is located on the west coast of Florida, approximately 65 miles north of St. Petersburg and 15 miles south of Crystal River.

https://www.fws.gov/refuge/chassahowitzka.

 The refuge was established in 1943 and consists of approximately 31,000 acres that include a mix of brackish waters, hardwood swamps, bays, and estuaries to the east with the Chassahowitzka River, a first magnitude spring system, spanning down to the southern border of the refuge. The Chass was one of my favorite places to kayak. The springs provide areas of crystal clear water with some underwater caves. I used to love swimming from one to the other. The northern border of the refuge runs alongside some of the Homosassa River.



 The refuge was originally established for the sake of providing an area for waterfowl such as coots and ducks. More recently, it has become a haven for the Florida manatee. The area is also an important feeding ground for these species due to the muskgrass, widgeongrass, and watermilfoil that grows in abundance. The swamp areas grow cypress, cedar, palm, and oak trees that provide necessary wildlife habitats. The mangrove trees at the coast line create much needed protection from incoming storms.

 Law enforcement is used to assist in the management of the refuge to help maintain and preserve these areas and prevent the destruction of these wildlife habitats. To ensure that these areas stay wild, over 75% of the refuge has been designated as uninhabitable wildlife areas.

 Further management techniques include prescribed fires every three to five years. The fire encourages growth of native vegetation by stimulating seed germination and allowing increased penetration of sunlight by clearing the understory and reducing brush. This process also releases many nutrients back into the soil.



Chassahowitzka National Wildlife Refuge. ( n.d.). U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service Chassahowitzka National Wildlife Refuge. https://www.fws.gov/sites/default/files/documents/ChassTearSheet16%20(5).pdf

W1, George Steward: Intro and Project Site Selection
George Steward posted Nov 6, 2024 7:57 AM•473 Words
PART 1: Introduction

Hey everyone! I’m George, and I’m joining the course from Austin, Texas, where my family and I have carved out our little slice of nature. When I&#x27;m not delving into ecosystem management, I&#x27;m usually immersed in homesteading and nurturing the land we live on. My family is my world: my teenager Ryder is deeply into wrestling and robotics, my 3-year-old toddler Evyn keeps us all on our toes, and our baby boy Bodie, who’s just 1.5 years old, is always full of wonder.

Currently, I’m building a business where I focus on ecological restoration and regenerative agriculture. My passion for the environment runs deep, and I’ve been in the green industry since my teenage years minus a few (14) years in the military. I also have a strong love for trees, bees, and the concept of biomimicry, and I’m always excited to explore the intricate connections between nature and human well-being. Taking this course is a natural step toward achieving my vision of blending technology with nature to create sustainable and resilient ecosystems.

What caught my attention in the syllabus was the emphasis on integrated conservation strategies. This ties in with my belief that we need to rethink how we live and manage our land, especially in the face of a climate that is shifting rapidly. I’m pursuing a degree in environmental science with the hope of consulting on ecological projects that balance the health of our planet with the needs of our communities.

PART 2: Selected Refuge

I’ve chosen to focus on the Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR) for my course project. This refuge is situated at the southern end of Puget Sound in Washington, near Olympia. This region has always captivated me. I’m fascinated by the Emerald Edge coastline, stretching from Oregon to Alaska, known for its incredible biodiversity, lush rainforests, and rich mycelial networks. The Nisqually Refuge embodies this region’s magic and resilience, and I’m intrigued by the idea that this area may be one of the last to be severely impacted as global temperatures rise.

One of the refuge&#x27;s most impressive feats is its extensive estuarine habitat restoration, which has reconnected nearly 700 acres of land to tidal flows, benefiting species like the threatened Chinook salmon. This work resonates with my passion for habitat restoration and the delicate balance between urban development and preserving natural landscapes. Nisqually also provides critical habitat for migratory birds along the Pacific Flyway, making it a vital area for research and conservation.

Here&#x27;s a map of the refuge to give you a sense of its stunning layout:
nisqually
[SOURCE] https://wos.org/documents/Birding%20Resources/NisquallyMap2014.pdf

I look forward to diving deep into the refuge’s Comprehensive Conservation Plan, exploring the management strategies, and sharing insights about this beautiful and ecologically significant place. You can learn more about Nisqually here.

Excited to connect and learn alongside all of you!

George

W1: Iroquois NWR - Schutz
Contains unread posts
Nicholas Schutz posted Nov 5, 2024 6:24 AMLast edited: Tuesday, November 5, 2024 8:36 AM EST
PART 1:

Everyone,

My name is Nicholas Schutz, and I am originally from Western New York. I currently live in Germany with my wife Kayla who is currently active-duty Air Force. We live with our two wonderful bundles of joy Merlin and Loki (cats who keep life &quot;interesting&quot;). We got here just over a month ago now and are still getting settled but we already love it. I am a full-time student currently but will be starting a job soon within the civil service system to keep me busy and hopefully start me on a path of career growth. I am an avid outdoorsman and fitness professional. I grew up outdoors hunting and fishing all around New York including the Adirondack Mountains State Park all the way over to Letchworth State Park. I am a CrossFit coach and Personal Trainer; I enjoy competing in fitness competitions and running long distance races. Combining these hobbies gets me outdoors and active just as I have been since childhood. Naturally, these also led me to pursue a degree in Environmental Science with a concentration in Wildlife and Fisheries Management. I hope to continue to grow in my knowledge of management practices and how hands on work within the field can control modern issues of Environmental Science. This degree path has led me to this course as one of my requirements for my major is Environmental and Ecosystem Management. Including the three courses I am taking currently; I am only eighteen credits away from my B.S. and I couldn&#x27;t be more excited to finish up. I hope to continue my education right into a master&#x27;s program. I hope to increase my opportunities to gain field experience in one of the four main Federal Land Management Agencies. I am specifically interested in the fish and wildlife criminal investigation aspect of public land management.

PART 2:

Choosing a National Wildlife Refuge for my project was easy to decide on as I can cover one close to home for me. The Iroquois National Wildlife Refuge in Basom, New York is thirty minutes from my childhood home, and I spent much of my time recreating in this area growing up. It is within the county that I also call home (Orleans County).

https://www.fws.gov/refuge/iroquois

I am using this Refuge as my focal point because it is home, but it is also an opportunity to discuss the uniqueness of New York and the beauty that the state offers. Most people I have met from my time in the military and international travels only think New York is the city and never consider the amazing diversity offered by the rest of the state.

Screenshot of Iroquois National Wildlife Refuge on the FWS NWRS website.

(Iroquois National Wildlife Refuge | Map Screenshot | U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service, 2024)

Established in 1958, the refuge was established with the goal in mind of allowing migratory waterfowl the opportunity to rest, feed, and breed (Iroquois National Wildlife Refuge | U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service, 2024). The Refuge is home to a diverse number of species from birds, mammals, fish, amphibians, to reptiles and invertebrates. The Summer season is a vital part of the Refuge as several species a water bird use the marshes as breeding grounds. Great blue herons, coots, and moorhens rely on the water for their population. Savannah Sparrows and meadowlarks include grassland birds that are also utilizing the fields of the refuge to breed. Songbirds are also abundant in the Summer as warblers accompany others along several trails on the Refuge to breed as well (Iroquois National Wildlife Refuge | U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service, 2024). Into the Fall season the migration of Mallard ducks and Canadian Geese utilize the marshes as staging areas as well. Spring and Winter offer great opportunities to observe Red-tailed hawks, red, and gray foxes hunting within the fields (Iroquois National Wildlife Refuge | U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service, 2024). Several environmental management tools are utilized to preserve the best habitat for a variety of fish and wildlife. The Iroquois National Wildlife Refuge is not a stranger to invasive species removal, prescribed burns, and inventory monitoring to conserve such a diverse ecosystem (Iroquois National Wildlife Refuge | U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service, 2024).

Resources:

Iroquois National Wildlife Refuge | U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2024). FWS.gov. https://www.fws.gov/refuge/iroquois/species

 

Iroquois National Wildlife Refuge | U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2024, July 5). FWS.gov. https://www.fws.gov/refuge/iroquois

 

Iroquois National Wildlife Refuge | Map Screenshot | U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2024, July 5). FWS.gov. https://www.fws.gov/refuge/iroquois

 

-Nick

Paul Wharton posted Nov 5, 2024 2:38 PMLast edited: Wednesday, November 6, 2024 10:55 AM EST
Good afternoon Professor and Class,  

Part I

My name is Paul Wharton.  I&#x27;m 41 years old and from Kentucky.  I retired this past June from active duty Navy, after over 21 years of service, and relocated to Las Vegas.  It&#x27;s a big blessing to have 3 children: 2 daughters at FAU and my son soon to transfer there as well.  IT’S THE FINAL COUNTDOWN!  Yes, I sang it aloud and thrilled to see the light ahead of my goal towards a BA in Environmental Science.  This course, along with the final three scheduled are program requirements and will wrap up by the end of February.  My undergraduate certification is in Regional and Community Planning.  School was on/off throughout active duty.  Now, there’s an opportunity to direct more focus on self and future goals.  My hobbies include anything outdoors.  I’ve enjoyed it that way since a little barefoot kid ripping and running through childhood.  Occasionally, I walk outside barefoot to ground myself and recharge with nature.  I also fly drones as both a hobby/commercial for aerial photography.  Been flying commercially for almost 3 years and recreation for about 9 years.  I’m currently employed with MSE Environmental, an environmental consulting and hygiene firm as a Lab Tech Analyst.  As the lab tech and Certified Mold Inspector, I conduct microscopic mold air sampling and analysis for various businesses and residential areas throughout Las Vegas.  We also perform testing for asbestos, lead, indoor air quality, and, GSA water testing.  I’ve been here almost 2 months and thoroughly enjoy my position and colleagues.  At MSE, there’s much to learn and new certification opportunities for increased scope of responsibility.  Working here offers a sense of pride in my occupation as I express the importance of human and environmental health, guidelines, and enforcement.  With that is my reason for choosing a degree aligning with who I am as a person.  I love to challenge myself and learning new things daily so I&#x27;m definitely looking forward to engaging in topic discussion with everyone throughout this course!



Part II

I selected Clarks River National Wildlife Refuge in Kentucky as my course project, it’s my home state https://ecos.fws.gov/ServCat/DownloadFile/19296.  This NWR site provides details of the state&#x27;s conservation efforts, allowing me to explore their vision and actions directed at maintaining a safe and healthy habitat while minimally interrupting natural resources with loss or contamination.  This project will provide a chance to look deeper into native species, their habitats, and habitability contributing to sustainable practices.  The wetlands within the refuge not only provide essential wildlife habitat but also play a crucial role in flood prevention for nearby cities and communities.  The conservation plan also addresses impacts such as deforestation, which leads to enhanced agriculture and urban development.  Bringing balance between land use and environmental stewardship is crucial for fostering a healthy and sustainable future for Clarks River Refuge.  Kentucky is known for migratory birds especially, many duck species and the establishment of waterfowl impoundments within Clark River.  The impoundments were created throughout over 200 acres of managed habitat for bird species.  The conservation strategy emphasized positive impacts when safeguarding bird species, habitats, and migration patterns.  It further encourages collaboration with landowners and includes economic incentives for wildlife habitat protection.  Agencies like the U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service and Kentucky&#x27;s state regulations share a heavy presence in assuring recreational activities are conducted in a manner outlining environmental conservation, education, and the importance of maintaining balanced ecosystems.



Clarks River National Wildlife Refuge - Ecos. (n.d.). https://ecos.fws.gov/ServCat/DownloadFile/19296 

Clarks River National Wildlife Refuge. FWS.gov. (2022, August 26). https://www.fws.gov/refuge/clarks-river 

I hope everyone had the chance to vote.  It matters, regardless affiliation.  These are rights we’re blessed to exercise and make differences.  Whomever your choice it’s okay and this guy has love for you, without judgment.  Have a great day, all!

Week 1-Sierra Barker
Contains unread posts
Sierra Barker posted Nov 8, 2024 8:31 PMLast edited: Saturday, November 9, 2024 6:16 AM EST
My name is Sierra Barker, and I Live in Pennsylvania near the Ohio border. I currently work at a youth center in my hometown. I am about halfway through a degree in environmental science with a focus on wildlife and fish.   In my spare time, I like to Garden, read (Mainly Fantasy but sometimes science fiction), play video games(like the Legend of Zelda series), hiking (my family and I just visited Yosemite recently), caring for animals, practicing archery, watching anime(My favorite is One Piece), and sew. I have 5 cats, 2 dogs, 4 ducks, 2 Turkeys, and 36 chickens.

            I&#x27;m taking this class because it is one of my major required courses. I hope to obtain some useful information from this class that I can use in my future career. After completing my degree, I would like to work as a park ranger, Conservationist, or zookeeper.

I have included a photo of my dogs Copper (Brown) and Lacey (Black), two of my cats Oliver(white) and Calvin(brown), Me and my turkey penguin, and a photo of my Bantam Boots sitting on one of my other chickens. 

           

 For this project I have decided to choose Okefenokee National wildlife Refuge Okefenokee National Wildlife Refuge | U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. I have read about this refuge before for an assignment in a different class so I thought it would be interesting to look into it.

The Okefenokee National Wildlife Refuge is one of the most remarkable and unique wetland ecosystems in the southeastern United States. This refuge, filled with dark, reflective waters and ancient cypress trees, is home to a range of species and habitats that make it an essential sanctuary for wildlife conservation (U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service, 2024).

One of the most iconic species here is the American alligator. These large reptiles play a vital role in the ecosystem by creating water-filled depressions that benefit countless other species, particularly during dry periods.

The refuge is also crucial for endangered species like the red-cockaded woodpecker. These birds rely on the longleaf pine forests found in upland areas of the Okefenokee. To support their habitat, refuge managers regularly conduct controlled burns, which help maintain the mature pine trees needed for nesting (U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service, 2024).

Besides the woodpecker and alligator, the swamp provides critical habitats for a range of other wildlife. Its prairie bogs and open wetlands are filled with unique plants like pitcher plants and sundews, which are adapted to the nutrient-poor soils here. These areas also attract wading birds like great blue herons, especially during migration periods. The cypress forests, with their haunting beauty, provide shelter for various fish, reptiles, and amphibians (U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service, 2024).

The Florida black bear also finds refuge here, using the swamp and surrounding forested areas for food, water, and shelter. This species serves as an umbrella species in the refuge, meaning that by protecting the bear&#x27;s habitat, many other species also benefit indirectly.



References
U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. (2024). Okefenokee National Wildlife Refuge. Retrieved from U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service: https://www.fws.gov/refuge/okefenokee

Zachary Minor posted Nov 10, 2024 5:11 PM
Hello Everyone!

My name is Zach, I am currently active duty in the Army, and stationed on the island of Oahu in Hawaii, which is where I will be residing for the entirety of this course. Outside of work and school, I enjoy spending time with my family, my wife and our daughter (who just turned recently turned 1). As a family we love to relax at home, go to the beach, go shopping, and taking long walks with our energetic husky. I also enjoy running, going to the gym, swimming, hiking, fishing, and more. And, Hawaii has been an awesome place to get to do all of these things, as well as explore some delicious food. The biggest adjustment to being here is the distance, the time differences, and the cost of living. 

I am very excited to be taking this course, as it is an important aspect I believe in my degree program, my future goals, and I am very eager to learn as much as I can. I am going for a bachelor&#x27;s degree in environmental science, with a concentration in wildlife management and conservation. From earning this degree, I hope to gain as much knowledge as possible, and eventually when I decide to part ways with the Army, I want to enter the environmental science field as a wildlife conservationist, wildlife officer, or in any other capacity. I am still researching more into each to decide what I want to pursue specifically. I am originally from Florida, therefore I decided to choose my project site based on this information. 

The site I selected for my project this semester is the St. Johns National Wildlife Refuge (St. Johns National Wildlife Refuge). This was established on August 16, 1971 and is in Brevard County, in east-central Florida. The refuge is 6,257 acres, and divided into two main management units:

SR 50 Unit
Bee Line Unit
St. Johns NWR

I chose this NWR because I grew up in this area, and spent many days on the St. Johns River, but I never researched much into the National Wildlife Refuge area in this area. It was established to protect threatened and endangered species, specifically the dusky seaside sparrow, which was federally endangered. Unfortunately, upon reading, I discovered that this species was officially declared extinct in 1990. However, the area still provides refuge for at least 19 federal and state listed species. Some of these species include wood stork, crested caracara, eastern indigo snake, and the American alligator. Along with these, seven nongame birds are listed in the refuge as species of management concern (barn owl, black rail, etc.). The area faces many issues including altered hydrological flows, poaching, invasive species, and more. The St. Johns NWR is not staffed, and is managed as a satellite of the Merritt Island NWR complex, which conducts the management activities on the St. Johns refuge. 

Dusky Seaside Sparrow

Dusky Seaside Sparrow which is now extinct (Pictured Above)

I have already learned so much about this NWR from research for this post, so I am quite eager to continue learning more as this project progresses throughout the course. I hope everyone had a great first week of this course, and I look forward to sharing this semester together.

-Zachary

W1, George Steward: Intro and Project Site Selection 
George Steward posted Nov 6, 2024 7:57 AM

PART 1: Introduction

Hey everyone! I’m George, and I’m joining the course from Austin, Texas, where my family and I have carved out our little slice of nature. When I&#x27;m not delving into ecosystem management, I&#x27;m usually immersed in homesteading and nurturing the land we live on. My family is my world: my teenager Ryder is deeply into wrestling and robotics, my 3-year-old toddler Evyn keeps us all on our toes, and our baby boy Bodie, who’s just 1.5 years old, is always full of wonder.

Currently, I’m building a business where I focus on ecological restoration and regenerative agriculture. My passion for the environment runs deep, and I’ve been in the green industry since my teenage years minus a few (14) years in the military. I also have a strong love for trees, bees, and the concept of biomimicry, and I’m always excited to explore the intricate connections between nature and human well-being. Taking this course is a natural step toward achieving my vision of blending technology with nature to create sustainable and resilient ecosystems.

What caught my attention in the syllabus was the emphasis on integrated conservation strategies. This ties in with my belief that we need to rethink how we live and manage our land, especially in the face of a climate that is shifting rapidly. I’m pursuing a degree in environmental science with the hope of consulting on ecological projects that balance the health of our planet with the needs of our communities.

PART 2: Selected Refuge

I’ve chosen to focus on the Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR) for my course project. This refuge is situated at the southern end of Puget Sound in Washington, near Olympia. This region has always captivated me. I’m fascinated by the Emerald Edge coastline, stretching from Oregon to Alaska, known for its incredible biodiversity, lush rainforests, and rich mycelial networks. The Nisqually Refuge embodies this region’s magic and resilience, and I’m intrigued by the idea that this area may be one of the last to be severely impacted as global temperatures rise.

One of the refuge&#x27;s most impressive feats is its extensive estuarine habitat restoration, which has reconnected nearly 700 acres of land to tidal flows, benefiting species like the threatened Chinook salmon. This work resonates with my passion for habitat restoration and the delicate balance between urban development and preserving natural landscapes. Nisqually also provides critical habitat for migratory birds along the Pacific Flyway, making it a vital area for research and conservation.

Here&#x27;s a map of the refuge to give you a sense of its stunning layout:
nisqually
[SOURCE] https://wos.org/documents/Birding%20Resources/NisquallyMap2014.pdf

I look forward to diving deep into the refuge’s Comprehensive Conservation Plan, exploring the management strategies, and sharing insights about this beautiful and ecologically significant place. You can learn more about Nisqually here.

Excited to connect and learn alongside all of you!

George

**ASSISTANT**:
To seamlessly integrate the discussions for both your Environmental and Ecosystem Management course and your other project on Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR), here’s a cohesive post for the pollution issues:

---

**Nisqually NWR (Steward)**

Hey everyone, for my final project on Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR), I&#x27;ve been diving into the pollution issues affecting this incredible ecosystem. Located at the southern end of Puget Sound in Washington, Nisqually is a vital area for migratory birds and marine species, but it&#x27;s not immune to environmental challenges.

**Types and Causes of Pollution:**

1. **Water Pollution**: One major issue stems from urban and agricultural runoff, which carries nutrients, pesticides, and other pollutants into the refuge’s estuarine waters. The nutrient overload can lead to algal blooms that deplete oxygen, threatening aquatic life. The nearby Nisqually River is impacted by sediment runoff, especially during storm events, which further compromises water quality.

2. **Marine Debris**: Plastics and other debris from Puget Sound make their way into the refuge, impacting wildlife that might ingest or become entangled in these materials. This pollution is exacerbated by waste from recreational activities and inadequate waste management in surrounding urban areas.

3. **Air Pollution**: Although less discussed, air pollution from vehicle emissions and industrial activities in nearby urban centers can affect plant health and water quality when airborne pollutants settle into the refuge.

**Management and Remedies:**

Nisqually NWR’s Comprehensive Conservation Plan (CCP) outlines a multi-faceted approach to tackling these pollution problems. Key strategies include:

- **Restoration Projects**: The 2009 estuarine restoration project, which reconnected nearly 700 acres to tidal flow, has been pivotal in improving water quality. The restoration not only enhances habitat resilience but also helps filter out pollutants.
- **Runoff Mitigation**: Efforts to establish buffer zones along the Nisqually River aim to reduce sediment and nutrient flow into the estuary. The refuge also collaborates with local farmers to implement best management practices that minimize agricultural runoff.
- **Public Education and Outreach**: Programs are in place to educate visitors on waste management and the importance of protecting these habitats.

**Reflection on Management:**

Overall, Nisqually NWR has been doing a commendable job, particularly with its large-scale estuarine restoration, which is considered one of the most significant in the Pacific Northwest. However, there’s always room for improvement. The refuge could benefit from expanded partnerships with local municipalities to address upstream pollution more aggressively. Initiatives like green infrastructure projects in nearby urban areas could further reduce runoff. Additionally, ramping up efforts to monitor and manage plastic pollution through community clean-up events and stricter regulations could make a meaningful impact.

**Why It Matters:**

These pollution challenges highlight the delicate balance Nisqually must maintain between preserving biodiversity and coexisting with human development. The refuge’s ability to manage these issues effectively is crucial not just for the local wildlife but for the overall health of the Puget Sound ecosystem, which supports thousands of species.

I’d love to hear your thoughts on how similar strategies could be applied to other NWRs or if you have additional ideas for tackling these pollution issues!

**References:**

- U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. (n.d.). Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge: Comprehensive Conservation Plan. Retrieved from https://www.fws.gov/refuge/nisqually
- Puget Sound Partnership. (2024). Addressing Urban and Agricultural Runoff. Retrieved from https://www.psp.wa.gov

![Nisqually Estuarine Restoration Map](insert-your-map-link-here)

*And a short video on the importance of maintaining clean waters in Puget Sound:*

[Watch Here](insert-your-video-link-here)

**Nisqually NWR (Steward)**

Hey everyone, sorry for jumping in a bit late here. Life’s been a bit chaotic, but I’m excited to share what I’ve learned so far about pollution issues at Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR). This place is pretty amazing, located down at the southern end of Puget Sound in Washington, near Olympia, and it’s vital for a ton of migratory birds and marine species. But, unfortunately, even this beautiful area isn’t immune to pollution problems.

**Types and Causes of Pollution:**

So, one big issue is **water pollution**. Urban and agricultural runoff carries a whole mix of nutrients, pesticides, and other pollutants into the estuarine waters. The extra nutrients can trigger these gnarly algal blooms, which then deplete oxygen levels and endanger aquatic species. Another problem is sediment runoff, especially when storms hit, eroding land along the Nisqually River and making water quality worse.

Another thing that’s on the radar is **marine debris**, mainly plastics and trash from Puget Sound. Wildlife gets tangled up in it or accidentally eats it, which is rough. The problem is made worse by people visiting the area and littering, plus the waste management in surrounding towns not being up to par.

And then there’s **air pollution**—it’s not as talked about, but emissions from vehicles and industrial activities nearby can settle onto the land and water, affecting plant life and the overall health of the habitat.

**Management and Remedies:**

Nisqually’s team has a lot going on to fight these issues. One of the coolest things is the 2009 estuarine restoration project, where they reconnected almost 700 acres of land to tidal flow. This was huge for improving water quality and habitat resilience, and it helps filter out pollutants. For runoff, they’ve been creating buffer zones along the Nisqually River to catch sediment before it gets into the water. Plus, they’re working with local farmers to reduce nutrient runoff using best practices.

They’ve also got some good public education programs to teach visitors about proper waste management and why it matters. 

**My Thoughts on Their Efforts:**

Honestly, I think they’re doing a solid job, especially with the massive restoration project. It’s pretty impressive. But like, there’s always more that could be done. Partnering more with local cities and towns to tackle upstream pollution would probably help a lot. Maybe they could even push for more green infrastructure projects to reduce runoff. And with plastic pollution, more frequent community clean-up events or stricter local regulations could be a game-changer.

At the end of the day, managing an area like Nisqually is all about balance. The refuge is a critical piece of the Puget Sound ecosystem, and keeping it clean is a big deal not just for wildlife but for the whole health of the region.

Would love to hear any ideas you all have on how these kinds of strategies could work elsewhere, or if you have any extra thoughts on what else they could do!

**References:**

- U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. (n.d.). Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge: Comprehensive Conservation Plan. Retrieved from https://www.fws.gov/refuge/nisqually
- Puget Sound Partnership. (2024). Addressing Urban and Agricultural Runoff. Retrieved from https://www.psp.wa.gov

*And a quick video about keeping Puget Sound waters clean:*

Lee Metcalf NWR (Castaneda)
Contains unread posts
Abel Castaneda posted Nov 12, 2024 5:39 PMLast edited: Wednesday, November 13, 2024 8:08 AM EST
Option 1

The Lee Metcalf National Wildlife Refuge (NWR) in Montana is a rich habitat facing diverse pollution challenges. The primary types of pollution include habitat fragmentation and invasive species encroachment. Key causes of these issues are human activities such as levee construction, water control measures, and invasive plant spread, all of which threaten the ecological balance of the refuge. The remedies being implemented include floodplain restoration, invasive species control through mechanical, chemical, and biological treatments, and native vegetation restoration (U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service, 2023).

The Refuge&#x27;s management approach aims to restore natural floodplain dynamics, vital for sustaining the Bitterroot River&#x27;s ecosystem. Historically, levees and other obstructions prevented the river from its natural seasonal flooding, essential for distributing nutrients and supporting native species. Current efforts involve potential levee removal or modification to enhance the river&#x27;s natural flow. This restoration is essential for floodplain habitats and, subsequently, for migratory birds, amphibians, and other wildlife that depend on a healthy riparian environment (U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service, 2024).

The invasive species problem, specifically with reed canary grass, further threatens native vegetation. Management&#x27;s strategy includes chemical treatments and planting native species that can eventually outcompete the invasive plants. This strategic restoration balances maintaining public access and promoting biodiversity, even in areas with a high influx of visitors (Howell, 2024). Additionally, the removal of a fish barrier at North Burnt Fork Creek will open 2.5 miles of stream habitat for migratory fish, including the threatened bull trout, improving the area&#x27;s ecological health and recreational fishing opportunities (U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service, 2023).

While Lee Metcalf NWR has made progress, more could be done to accelerate restoration efforts. Increasing funding and partnerships with local conservation groups could enhance invasive species control and support more extensive floodplain restoration. Additionally, educating the public on the significance of these efforts can foster community involvement, furthering conservation goals (Howell, 2024). Overall, the Refuge&#x27;s management has laid a solid foundation, but these additional steps could bolster its mission of conservation and ecological integrity.

References

Howell, M. (2024, September 10). Fish barrier being eliminated on North Burnt Fork Creek. Bitterroot Star. Retrieved from https://bitterrootstar.com/2024/09/fish-barrier-being-eliminated-on-north-burnt-fork-creek

U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2023, January 5). Lee Metcalf National Wildlife Refuge releases draft environmental assessment for fish passage barrier removal. Retrieved from https://www.fws.gov/press-release/2023-01/lee-metcalf-national-wildlife-refuge-releases-draft-environmental-assessment

U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service. (2024). Lee Metcalf National Wildlife Refuge: What we do. Retrieved from https://www.fws.gov/refuge/lee-metcalf/what-we-do

Stauffer Chemical Co., FL
Contains unread posts
Brook Caban posted Nov 15, 2024 3:51 AMLast edited: Friday, November 15, 2024 3:51 AM EST
Hello Class! 

The superfund site I decided to go with is called Stauffer Chemical Co. and is in Tarpon Springs, FL. I chose this site because I am from Land O&#x27; Lakes, FL. My town is not too far from Tarpon Springs and growing up I would go there all the time for the BEST Greek food around.  

This site is owned by Stauffer Management Company and was added to the NPL in 1994 and has not been removed. It is still being monitored and worked on to prevent any more pollution. Before it closed in 1983, they would make elemental phosphorous from phosphate ore that was mined nearby. There was so much phosphorus dust everywhere that it would cause constant fires, and the areas nearby would be covered in tar.  After testing the water and soil in the area there were traces of &quot;elemental phosphorus, arsenic, antimony, beryllium, thallium, and radium-226&quot; (Stauffer Chemical Co., n.d.). To try fixing this they created a wall to cut off the flow of the water to stop the pollution from continuing to contaminate the rest of the ground water essentially stopping runoff. The soil at the site was also capped off to stop pollution from spreading. They removed as much pollution as possible, restricting it to specific areas, and there were laws set in place where they aren&#x27;t allowed to build houses at the site or use the groundwater to prevent any further harm to the people in the area. They were able to do this by putting up barriers around the site as well, to stop anyone from going near it. They are still monitoring the site and cleaning as much as they can to protect the people in the Tarpon Springs area.  

This is the map of the Stauffer Chemical Co. superfund site 



This is a picture of what the water cutoff wall looks like

The Marzone Inc./Chevron Chemical Co, GA
Contains unread posts
Carlee Shaw posted Nov 14, 2024 2:39 PM
Hello Class!

First, I apologize for getting this posted late. Second, I wanted to talk about a superfund site here in Georgia, as there weren&#x27;t too many post about superfunds yet. The Marzone Inc./Chevron Chemical Co. superfund site is located in Tifton, Georgia. 



Chevron Chemical Company owned and operated a pesticide and herbicide formulation plant on a portion of the site.  Chevron Chemical operated on the site from 1950 to 1970. Several other companies, including Marzone Chemical Company, owned and operated the plant from 1970 until 1983. From the late 1960s until 1992, different companies, including Golden Seed Inc., operated a formulation and packaging plant for pesticides and fertilizers on another portion of the site.

In 1989, EPA added the site to the Superfund Program&#x27;s National Priorities List(NPL).  Plant operations resulted in contamination of the soil, sediment and groundwater.

EPA divides sites into Operable Units (OUs) to make cleanup activities more efficient. 

Contaminants of concern (COCs) in OU-1 surface soil, below-surface soil and groundwater include atrazine, alpha hexachlorocyclohexane (HCH) (formally known as benzene hexachloride, or BHC), beta HCH, dichlorodiphenyldichloroethane (DDD), dichlorodiphenyldichloroethylene (DDE), dichlorodiphenyltrichloroethane (DDT), dieldrin, endosulfan II, ethylbenzene, heptachlor epoxide, lindane, methyl parathion, toxaphene and xylene.

COCs in OU-2 surface soil and sediment include DDT, DDE, DDD, toxaphene, alpha chlordane, gamma chlordane, copper, lead and zinc. COCs in OU-2 ground water include aluminum, beryllium, cadmium, manganese, nickel, lead, manganese, iron, nitrate/nitrite, alpha HCH, gamma HCH, endrin and dinoseb.

EPA completed the fourth Five-Year Review in July 2017. It found that the cleanup at OU-1 and OU-2 continues to protect people and the environment in the short term. The remedy currently protects human health and the environment because contaminated soil and sediments have been excavated and replaced with clean fill and vegetated. For site remedies to be protective over the long term, additional groundwater institutional controls are necessary and enhancement of the groundwater remedies may be needed.

https://cumulis.epa.gov/supercpad/SiteProfiles/index.cfm?fuseaction=second.cleanup&amp;id=0401854

Carlee

Chassahowitzka NWR - Dugan
Contains unread posts
Darlene Dugan posted Nov 13, 2024 1:13 PMLast edited: Wednesday, November 13, 2024 3:36 PM EST
Since I really need to fall back into a groove with school, I decided to begin working on my final. This will be the topic of my post for week 2.

The Chassahowitzka NWR has a number of of pollution issues, but the primary is issue is water quality of the springs and rivers.

Chass

These areas suffer from nitrate enrichment which poses a number of problems for many different species. Nitrate enrichment is caused by agricultural and residential fertilizers, urban development, and underground waste treatment, such as septic tanks. Additionally, wastes are washed into waterways from many roads in the area through storm water and groundwater contributions.

Over enrichment causes a nutrient imbalance that causes eutrophication promoting excessive growth of micro algae. This algae smothering kills plant life by reducing exposure to sunlight that submerged aquatic vegetation relies on. It reduces visibility and raises the pH of the water. Some species of algae can produce toxins that cause harm to animals, plant life, and humans.

Runoff

Changing salinity also affects water quality and is a concern for these areas. Salinity changes can be caused by sea level rise and major storms along the coast such as hurricanes. Salinity levels are ever changing. Some specific areas of in the Chassahowitzka NWR constantly change from brackish water back to fresh water due to water flows.

A number of management strategies have been implemented in order to mitigate the effects of pollution. Minimum flows and levels (MFL&#x27;s) help to plan for our current and future water needs. It helps to offset groundwater use with projects that require permitting for water withdrawal in these areas. Additional programs encourage the conservation of water and provide alternative sourcing.

The Florida Department of Environmental Protection under the Florida Springs and Aquifer Protection Act has established changes to wastewater disposal facilities, made improvements to onsite septic systems and created new facilities for hazardous wastes. Additionally, they have implemented a fertilizer ordinance, a pet waste ordinance, and provide ongoing public education. They have also redirected inadequate routes of wastewater flows. They have created and encouraged the use of reclaimed water systems in residential areas as well as successfully promoted the use of reclaimed water irrigation systems for golf courses in the area. 

The Southwest Florida Water Management District, the Florda Department of Environmental Protection and Florida work with Florida Fish and Wildlife to continually monitor, assess the needs of the Chassahowitzka NWR.

Chass river system

 

 

Florida Department of Environmental Protection, Valenstein, N., Homosassa and Chassahowitzka Stakeholders, &amp; Hansen, T. (2018). Homosassa and Chassahowitzka Springs Groups Basin Management Action Plan (pp. 2–98).
Southwest Florida Water Management District. (n.d.). Minimum Flows and Levels (MFLs).https://www.swfwmd.state.fl.us/projects/mfls

Nisqually NWR (Steward)
George Steward posted Nov 16, 2024 7:25 AM•536 Words
Nisqually NWR (Steward)

Hey everyone, sorry for jumping in a bit late here. Life’s been a bit chaotic, but I’m excited to share what I’ve learned so far about pollution issues at Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR). This place is pretty amazing. It is located down at the southern end of Puget Sound in Washington, near Olympia, and it’s vital for many migratory birds and marine species. Unfortunately, this beautiful area isn’t immune to pollution problems.

Types and Causes of Pollution:

So, one big issue is water pollution. Urban and agricultural runoff carries a whole mix of nutrients, pesticides, and other pollutants into the estuarine waters. The extra nutrients can trigger these gnarly algal blooms, which then deplete oxygen levels and endanger aquatic species. Another problem is sediment runoff, especially when storms hit, eroding land along the Nisqually River and making water quality worse.

Another thing that’s on the radar is marine debris, mainly plastics and trash from Puget Sound. Wildlife gets tangled up in it or accidentally eats it, which is rough. The problem is made worse by people visiting the area and littering, plus the waste management in surrounding towns not being up to par.

And then there’s air pollution—it’s not as talked about, but emissions from vehicles and industrial activities nearby can settle onto the land and water, affecting plant life and the overall health of the habitat.

Management and Remedies:

Nisqually’s team has a lot going on to fight these issues. One of the coolest things is the 2009 estuarine restoration project, where they reconnected almost 700 acres of land to tidal flow. This was huge for improving water quality and habitat resilience, and it helps filter out pollutants. For runoff, they’ve been creating buffer zones along the Nisqually River to catch sediment before it gets into the water. Plus, they’re working with local farmers to reduce nutrient runoff using best practices.

They also have good public education programs to teach visitors about proper waste management and why it matters.

My Thoughts on Their Efforts:

I think they’re doing a solid job, especially with the massive restoration project. It’s pretty impressive. But there’s always more that could be done. Partnering more with local cities and towns to tackle upstream pollution would probably greatly help. Maybe they could even push for more green infrastructure projects to reduce runoff. And with plastic pollution, more frequent community clean-up events or stricter local regulations could be a game-changer.

Managing an area like Nisqually is about balance using a multi-faceted approach. The refuge is a critical piece of the Puget Sound ecosystem, and keeping it clean is a big deal not just for wildlife but for the whole region’s health.

I would love to hear any ideas you have on how these kinds of strategies could work elsewhere, or if you have any extra thoughts on what else they could do!

References:

U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. (n.d.). Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge: Comprehensive Conservation Plan. Retrieved from https://www.fws.gov/refuge/nisqually 

Puget Sound Partnership. (2024). Addressing Urban and Agricultural Runoff. Retrieved from https://www.psp.wa.gov
