Why I Must Speak Out About Climate Change (Hansen)
Contains unread posts
Carlee Shaw posted Nov 27, 2024 4:32 PMLast edited: Thursday, November 28, 2024 8:45 AM EST
Hello Class,

James Hansen, a renowned climate scientist, presents this talk. He served as the head of NASA&#x27;s Goddard Institute for Space Studies for over 30 years and is a pioneer in climate change research. His expertise lies in climate modeling and studying the effects of greenhouse gases on Earth&#x27;s atmosphere, making him highly qualified to discuss the topic. 



Talk Details: Delivered in February 2012, this TED talk highlights the overwhelming evidence for climate change and Hansen&#x27;s concerns about the future. He calls for immediate action, explaining the science of climate change and urging policy changes to reduce carbon emissions. The full talk can be found on the TED website.

Relevance and Updates: While the talk remains foundational, the science has advanced since 2012. Key developments include more precise models predicting extreme weather patterns and discussions about climate equity. Hansen&#x27;s emphasis on carbon emissions remains vital, but modern discussions might also include renewable energy adoption, climate resilience, and climate migration issues.

Grade: The talk earns an &quot;A&quot; for its clarity, scientific rigor, and Hansen&#x27;s ability to distill complex information into actionable insights. Its slight drawback is that newer developments in climate policy and science are not addressed, given its age.

Carlee

James Hansen&#x27;s TED Talk

Global Movement to Restore Biodiversity (Crowther)
George Steward posted Nov 27, 2024 1:40 PM•457 WordsLast edited: Thursday, November 28, 2024 8:45 AM EST
Presenter and Qualifications
Dr. Thomas Crowther, an ecosystem ecologist and professor at ETH Zurich, gave this talk in 2020. His work, including the landmark study mapping global tree density, has reshaped our understanding of the planet&#x27;s ecosystems. Dr. Crowther&#x27;s commitment to restoring biodiversity is reflected in his leadership of the Crowther Lab, which works at the cutting edge of ecological restoration. I&#x27;ve had the privilege of connecting with people who&#x27;ve studied under Dr. Crowther. One colleague works with SPUN, a non-profit dedicated to mapping mycorrhizal fungal networks, and Funga, a company focused on forest fungi inoculation. These efforts align beautifully with Dr. Crowther&#x27;s message of restoration as a collaborative, biodiversity-centered movement.

Talk Overview and Relevance
This talk is a heartfelt call to action. Dr. Crowther reminds us that ecosystems, and the biodiversity within them, are not just tools for fighting climate change—they are life itself. He discusses the potential of restoring nearly a trillion trees to capture decades of human emissions while healing the Earth&#x27;s natural systems. His candid acknowledgment of past communication missteps only adds to the sincerity of his message. While the talk remains highly relevant, I&#x27;d love to hear updates on how tools like Restor and collaborations with groups like SPUN are scaling these efforts.

Grading the Talk: A
This talk deserves an A for its ability to inspire action. Crowther combines cutting-edge science with a deeply human message: that we must protect what remains and restore what has been lost. His work offers hope at a time when it&#x27;s sorely needed. The only reason this isn&#x27;t an A+ is the limited discussion on how restoration projects can navigate socio-political hurdles, especially in regions where ecosystems are under intense pressure.

Reflection
What resonates most is the profound connection between biodiversity and human well-being. As someone who&#x27;s spent time studying and working in regenerative systems, I found Crowther&#x27;s emphasis on local ecosystems and indigenous knowledge incredibly grounding. Restoration is about more than trees; it&#x27;s about nurturing the intricate, interwoven relationships of life on Earth. Knowing that groups like SPUN are mapping fungal networks and Funga is bringing forest fungi back into the fold shows how this movement is evolving into something even more inclusive and powerful.

Happy Thanksgiving, I am grateful for the natural world and the humans who dedicate themselves to caring for it.



Thomas Crowther: The global movement to restore nature&#x27;s biodiversity

References:

Crowther, T. (2020, October). The global movement to restore nature&#x27;s biodiversity [Video]. TED Conferences. https://www.ted.com/talks/thomas_crowther_the_global_movement_to_restore_nature_s_biodiversity

Society for the Protection of Underground Networks (SPUN). (n.d.). Protect the underground. https://www.spun.earth/

Funga. (n.d.). Harnessing forest fungal networks to address the climate crisis. https://www.funga.earth/

Crowther Lab. (n.d.). Global ecosystem ecology. https://crowtherlab.com/

Restor. (n.d.). A platform to support and connect the global restoration movement. https://restor.eco/

Tipping Points of Climate Change (Rockstrom)
Contains unread posts
Joseph Mazik posted Nov 26, 2024 12:22 PMLast edited: Thursday, November 28, 2024 8:48 AM EST
Class,

Johan Rockstrom, a prominent Earth system scientist, delivered this talk. He serves as the Director of the Potsdam Institute for Climate Impact Research and is a Professor in Earth System Science at the University of Potsdam. Rockstrom is internationally recognized for his work on global sustainability and Earth resilience. He led the development of the Planetary Boundaries framework, which identifies nine critical processes essential for maintaining the stability of Earth&#x27;s systems. 



The talk was delivered in July 2024. Given its recent presentation, the information is current and reflects the latest scientific findings and climate data available at that time. However, the rapidly evolving nature of climate science means that continuous updates are necessary to incorporate new research, advancements, and policy.

The grade of B+ reflects the talk&#x27;s effectiveness in conveying complex scientific concepts related to climate tipping points in a simple manner. Rockstrom&#x27;s expertise and ease of communication highlight the urgency of addressing climate change. I think the talk could have benefited from a more detailed section on actionable solutions and strategies for mitigation and adaptation. It is always recommended to provide the audience with concrete steps to address the challenges presented.

Joe

References

TED. (2024). The tipping points of climate change—and where we stand. Retrieved from https://www.ted.com/talks/johan_rockstrom_the_tipping_points_of_climate_change_and_where_we_stand

Potsdam Institute for Climate Impact Research. (n.d.). Prof. Dr. Johan Rockstrom. Retrieved from https://www.pik-potsdam.de/members/johanro

Steffen, W., Richardson, K., Rockstrom, J., et al. (2015). Planetary boundaries: Guiding human development on a changing planet. Science, 347(6223). DOI: 10.1126/science.1259855

Climate Change Slowing Down the Ocean (Lozier)
Contains unread posts
Kelly Orndorff posted Nov 26, 2024 6:33 PMLast edited: Thursday, November 28, 2024 8:47 AM EST

         This week I chose &quot;Is Climate Change Slowing Down the Ocean?&quot; presented by Susan Lozier. Susan is the dean of the College of Sciences and a professor in Earth and Atmospheric Sciences at Georgia Institute of Technology (Susan Lozier, 2024). She is also a physical oceanographer concentrating on how the ocean plays a part in warming temperatures and climate variability. Susan was the President of the American Geophysical Union which is a global professional organization of earth and space scientist and is currently a board member of the University Corporation for Atmospheric Research. Her current work has her leading the program Overturing in the Subpolar North Atlantic (OSNAP) which is a system to observe and measure air motion in the subpolar North Atlantic (Susan Lozier, 2024). She is very well qualified to present this TED talk.
        Lozier gave her presentation in July of 2023. She begins by explaining ocean overturning circulation and how it brings warm water to the surface and when it cools it becomes denser and skinks along with the carbon dioxide it had absorbed. This carbon dioxide had increased the oceans acidic level (Lozier, 2023) The National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration confirmed that information. The concern is that the combination of rising temperature and ice melting will reduce the density of surface water which eventually will lead to the surface water not sinking causing the ocean overturning circulation to slow down (Lozier, 2023). In turn this will create more extreme temperatures in the poles and by the equator and increase weather intensity. This information I found to be true as well according to What the Slowdown of Atlantic Ocean Circulation means for the Future of the Climate (2024). Lozier refers to the study of OSNAP and what they are measuring which is the program she is still working on currently. 
        I would give this an A. The talk was presented slowly and in terms that were easy to understand. Susan started by explaining the area of concern with photos and graphs that were each explained in detail. I think this is important when talking to a big audience because everyone needs to fully comprehend what the speaker is talking about right at the beginning. Next was her area of expertise and she gave studies, charts, and facts to back up the concern. She then moved on to giving the hypothesis of what will happen if nothing changes on a global scale. The end global research that was still being carried out put perspective on how important the issue is and gave the audience more evidence and credentials of the speakers&#x27; work. I really enjoyed listening to her. 
References:
Department of Commerce. (2024, July 16). What is the AMOC? NOAA&#x27;s National Ocean Service. https://oceanservice.noaa.gov/facts/amoc.html

Department of Commerce. (2020, April 1). Ocean Acidification. National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration.https://www.noaa.gov/education/resource-collections/ocean-coasts/ocean-acidification

Escueta, C. (2024, February 1). What the Slowdown of Atlantic Ocean Circulation means for the Future of the Climate. Earth.Org. https://earth.org/atlantic-ocean-circulation-slowdown/

Georgia Institute of Technology. (2024). Susan Lozier. https://lozier.eas.gatech.edu/

Lozier, S. (2023, July). Is Climate Change Slowing Down the Ocean? TED Talk. https://www.ted.com/talks/susan_lozier_is_climate_change_slowing_down_the_ocean?subtitle=en

This country....it&#x27;s carbon negative (Tobgay)
Contains unread posts
Kimberly Wolf posted Nov 27, 2024 8:26 PMLast edited: Thursday, November 28, 2024 8:44 AM EST


I watched the Ted talk given by Tshering Tobgay, in February 2016. Tshering Tobgay was the President of the People&#x27;s Democratic Party in Bhutan. Bhutan is the first nation in the world to be carbon neutral and to take it a step farther they are the first country in the world to become carbon negative. It is very impressive and restores faith that there are governments that want to do everything in their power to ensure the happiness and wellbeing of all of their citizens as well as doing what they can to help address world problems such as climate change. 

He talks about his countries priority to address the gross national happiness instead of just economic growth. They have an approach which he calls holistic in that they carefully balance economic growth with social issues such as social development, free education and free university. They are working hard towards environmental sustainability with provisions in their constitution that demand environmental protection and sustainability with clean energy goals and the constitutional requirement that 60% of the country must remain in forest cover. It is beautiful and ambitious, and I wish that more countries could follow suit. They are fighting for all of this while still balancing the economy and working towards cultural preservation. It&#x27;s so beautiful. 

 The Ted talk information is still relevant in that it is impressive even with it being almost ten years old. Since then, Bhutan has not only maintained their ambitious goals they have out done them. They are projected to offset 22.4 million tons of CO2 in 2025 by hydroelectric exports. At the time they were the only nation to achieve this carbon negative goal, but they have since been joined by Suriname, a small country north of Brazil, and Panama, a country at the southern tip of Central America (Climate &amp; Clean Air Coalition, n.d.). 

I was so shocked by the pictures he shared of the glaciers in his country. He gave another Ted talk in 2019 where he talks about the melting of the third-largest repository of ice, after the North and South Poles in the Hindu Kush Himalaya region.

 


 Kimberly Wolf


Climate &amp; Clean Air Coalition. (n.d.). Panama. https://www.ccacoalition.org/partners/panama

We can&#x27;t solve the climate crisis without cows (Costain)
Contains unread posts
Kyle Bruce posted Nov 27, 2024 10:22 AMLast edited: Thursday, November 28, 2024 8:46 AM EST


&quot;We Can&#x27;t Solve the Climate Crisis Without Cows&quot; - Costain
Presenter and Qualifications
This TEDx talk is presented by ffinlo Costain, a regenerative agriculture advocate and host of the podcast Farm Gate. Costain is also the Chief Executive of the organization Farmwel, which works to accelerate the transition to sustainable farming systems. His expertise lies in agricultural policy, ecological restoration, and sustainability, making him well-qualified to discuss the intersection of livestock management and climate change.

Talk Information
The talk was given in 2022 at TEDxYouth. While the overall message of the talk remains relevant, some newer advancements in methane management and updates on international agricultural policies could further enrich the discussion. For instance, advancements in feed additives that reduce methane emissions or policies focused on carbon sequestration incentives could be included in an updated version of this talk.

Reflection
The talk challenges the common misconception that cows are a primary driver of climate change. Costain explains how regenerative farming systems leverage livestock to enhance soil health, increase biodiversity, and sequester carbon. His argument highlights the nuanced role of methane as a short-lived greenhouse gas compared to carbon dioxide from fossil fuels, providing a balanced view of livestock&#x27;s environmental impact.

Grade: A-
I gave this talk an A- because:

Strengths:
Costain presents a fresh perspective, countering mainstream narratives with evidence-based solutions.
The explanation of regenerative grazing and its benefits for climate mitigation is compelling and easy to understand.
He effectively uses humor and relatable examples, such as likening cows to biodigesters, to engage the audience.
Limitations:
While informative, the talk could include more specific examples of policies or technologies advancing regenerative agriculture.
Some concepts, like hydroxyl ion production, could benefit from additional scientific clarification for general audiences.
Reference:
Costain, f. (2022). We can&#x27;t solve the climate crisis without cows [TEDx talk]. TEDxYouth@ShaftesburySchool. Retrieved from https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=O9Ed84v_C5k

Lessons from the Past on Adapting...(Daniels)
Contains unread posts
Nicholas Schutz posted Nov 28, 2024 2:50 AMLast edited: Thursday, November 28, 2024 8:42 AM EST
Everyone, 

I decided to analyze the TED Talk Lessons from the Past on Adapting to Climate Change by Laprisha Berry Daniels. Daniels utilizes her professional experience as a Public Health Social Worker to highlight her endeavour to develop interventions to improve community resilience and health. Her particular focus is aimed towards addressing the harms that occur during a crisis. She also further enhanced her speech and connection with the audience by using her family&#x27;s personal experiences during the Great Migration of the 1950s where she was able to pull lessons from her grandparents&#x27; experiences. This offered her the opportunity to connect modern strategies to adapt with historical cases (S, 2024). This speech was presented during the TED Countdown Summit in 2023 and published in 2024. The information and topics covered by Daniels are up to date as she addresses the modern issues facing humanity due to climate change. Community resilience and preparedness, extreme weather events, and crisis management. Daniels continued to address the difference between the two definitions of climate (weather vs. social) to highlight the necessity to adapt how we approach &quot;climate&quot; issues on both fronts (Daniels, 2024). I would grade this speech as an A- due to the effectiveness of her storytelling and insight about climate adaptation. She expertly made historical and personal connections to broaden the reach of her message. It also highlighted climate adaptation rather than climate change mitigation or other environmental topics that are more prevalent. I would have only heard some more detailed examples of innovative technologies and systematic adaptations that could help to curb the crisis. 

-Nick

Most important thing you can do...(Hayhoe)
Contains unread posts
Sierra Barker posted Nov 26, 2024 10:55 PMLast edited: Thursday, November 28, 2024 8:46 AM EST
Presenter and Qualifications:

The ted talk that I chose is presented by Dr. Katharine Hayhoe, who is a renowned atmospheric scientist, climate change expert, and professor at Texas Tech University. She is also the Chief Scientist for The Nature Conservancy and has written multiple publications on climate science, policy, and public engagement. Dr. Hayhoe is qualified to speak on this topic due to her academic background, research contributions, and her ability to communicate complex scientific concepts to non-scientific audiences.

Date of the Talk:
This TED Talk was given in November of 2018.

Content of the Talk:

Dr. Hayhoe talks about the importance of open and honest conversations about climate change as a means of addressing the crisis. She argues that there are people that are aware of the issue but feel paralyzed by how complicated it is or skeptical due to incorrect info. Dr. Hayhoe talks on how personal, relatable discussions rooted in shared values can break down these barriers.

The talk mainly focuses on how effective communication can help inspire action by connecting climate science with people&#x27;s everyday lives, such as their health, local environment, or faith. She challenges the audience to engage in constructive conversation with friends, family, and communities instead of avoiding the subject. Her goal is to show that anyone can advocate for solutions, even without being a scientist.

Reflection on the Information&#x27;s Relevance:


The talk is still relevant because it addresses a important issue: the social and psychological barriers to addressing climate change. However, including advancements in climate science, renewable energy, and public policy could add depth to her message. If updated, Dr. Hayhoe might discuss:

New evidence of climate impacts, such as record-breaking weather events.
Advances in clean energy solutions, like improved battery technology.
The role of youth-led climate movements, such as Fridays for Future, in sparking action.
The increasing prevalence of misinformation campaigns and how to counter them effectively.
Grade

I believe Dr. Katharine Hayhoe&#x27;s TED Talk earns an &quot;A&quot; for its clarity, relatability, and practical impact. She effectively communicates climate science in a way that is understandable to a general audience. The talk addresses a critical global issue by focusing on the power of communication and dialogue as tools to promote action, offering advice that allows individuals to engage meaningfully on climate change.

While this ted talk was delivered in 2018, its message about the importance of conversations on shared values remains relevant. The only minor limitation is the lack of recent data or developments, but this does not detract from the foundational strength of the talk.

References
Hayhoe, K. (2018, 11). The most important thing you can do to fight climate change: talk about it. Retrieved from TED.com: https://www.ted.com/talks/katharine_hayhoe_the_most_important_thing_you_can_do_to_fight_climate_change_talk_about_it/transcript?subtitle=en

10 Years to Transform Humanity (Rockstrom)
Contains unread posts
Trevor Miller posted Nov 24, 2024 6:21 PMLast edited: Tuesday, November 26, 2024 11:52 AM EST
Hello everyone,

We are officially at the halfway point, not that I&#x27;m counting or anything. This week we are discussing climate change. I found a lot of TEDX talks, but the one that stuck out to me was 10 Years to Transform Humanity by Rockstrom



So who is Johan Rockstrom? Johan Rockström, a leading climate scientist and director of the Potsdam Institute for Climate Impact Research, delivered this TED talk. He co-developed the planetary boundaries framework, which defines critical thresholds for Earth&#x27;s systems to sustain life. His expertise in climate resilience and sustainability makes him a credible and authoritative voice on the subject.

This TEDX talk was presented in October 2020, the talk remains relevant for its core message about the urgency of addressing climate tipping points. However, newer data, such as updates on ice sheet melting, extreme weather events, and the accelerated adoption of renewable energy, might shift the focus today. Since 2020, additional international efforts, such as COP26 agreements, and technological advancements in decarbonization could enrich this discussion.

Rockstrom highlights that nine of the 15 Earth systems, such as the Amazon rainforest and Arctic ice, are nearing tipping points. He emphasizes a 10-year window to stabilize these systems, offering hope through science-backed solutions. A current talk might delve deeper into post-2020 progress in these areas, such as breakthroughs in clean energy or challenges in international cooperation.

The grade I gave was an &quot;A&quot;. This grade reflects Rockstrom&#x27;s ability to make complex science understandable through vivid visuals and actionable solutions. His balance of urgency and optimism effectively engages a wide audience, inspiring meaningful climate action. 

I was quite surprised this week with finding the TEDX talks. Simply put, there was a shocking amount of information to digest - most of which was quite disturbing if we are being honest.

I look forward to seeing what everyone else does their posts on this week.

-TA

References:

Rockström, J. (2020, October). 10 years to transform the future of humanity -- or destabilize the planet [Video]. TED Conferences. https://www.ted.com/talks/johan_rockstrom_10_years_to_transform_the_future_of_humanity_or_destabilize_the_planet

Potsdam Institute for Climate Impact Research. (n.d.). Johan Rockström: Director and climate impact scientist. Retrieved November 24, 2024, from https://www.pik-potsdam.de

Sustainability Speakers. (n.d.). Johan Rockström – Climate change expert and sustainability speaker. Retrieved November 24, 2024, from https://sustainability-speakers.com/speaker/johan-rockstrom

**ASSISTANT**:
### Suggested Posts for Commenting

#### 1. **Abel Castaneda: Climate Solutions Worth Funding Now (Foley)**
Abel’s post aligns with the sentiment of your own work on biodiversity and ecological restoration. While Dr. Foley emphasizes practical solutions for reducing emissions, you can connect this to the broader message of restoring biodiversity as foundational to climate resilience. You could elaborate on how biodiversity-based restoration efforts like rewilding or using the Miyawaki Method complement the solutions Dr. Foley discusses.

**Comment Idea:**  
&quot;Abel, I really appreciate your focus on Dr. Foley&#x27;s actionable solutions. It resonates with the broader message that addressing climate change requires more than just mitigation—it needs ecosystem restoration at its core. Have you considered how biodiversity-focused approaches, like rewilding or dense afforestation using the Miyawaki Method, can enhance these climate solutions? Biodiversity supports carbon sequestration and offers additional benefits like water regulation and soil health, which Foley might have touched on in a more extended discussion. What do you think about blending these approaches to create holistic solutions?&quot;

---

#### 2. **Kyle Bruce: We Can&#x27;t Solve the Climate Crisis Without Cows (Costain)**
Kyle’s post offers an opportunity to challenge the overemphasis on livestock in addressing climate change. While regenerative agriculture is important, you could argue that restoration efforts focusing on natural vegetation and biodiversity may offer more long-term, scalable benefits compared to livestock management.

**Comment Idea:**  
&quot;Kyle, I found your post thought-provoking, especially Costain&#x27;s perspective on livestock&#x27;s role in regenerative agriculture. While I agree that cows can play a part in improving soil health and sequestering carbon, I wonder if the focus on livestock might overshadow the importance of rewilding efforts. Restoring native ecosystems, like forests or grasslands, offers a more scalable, long-term solution to biodiversity loss and climate change. Have you considered how prioritizing natural vegetation restoration, rather than relying heavily on livestock, might align with Costain’s goals while reducing methane emissions at the source? I&#x27;d love to hear your thoughts on balancing these approaches.&quot;

Let me know if you’d like help drafting or refining these comments further!

Floating Treatment Wetland Systems (FTWS)
Contains unread posts
Abel Castaneda posted Dec 3, 2024 8:33 PMLast edited: Thursday, December 5, 2024 10:52 AM EST
I opted for option two when I saw my CCP did not address waste management solutions or programs.

In Nepal&#x27;s Nagdaha Lake, hydrologist Soni Pradhanang has transformed pollution into opportunity with Floating Treatment Wetland Systems (FTWS) made from local waste materials. These 4-by-6-foot platforms, constructed using recycled styrofoam, bamboo mats, and coconut fiber, serve as cost-effective water-cleaning devices. Topped with flowering plants like Indian shot and scarlet sage, the platforms absorb pollutants such as nitrates, phosphates, and heavy metals, while their roots release oxygen, revitalizing aquatic ecosystems (Richter, 2024).

This innovative approach, dubbed &quot;trash for cash,&quot; addresses two major environmental challenges: waste recycling and water purification. In two years, the FTWS clusters in Nagdaha have demonstrated impressive results, including a 99% reduction in nitrates and significant decreases in phosphates, iron, and ammonia. The aesthetic improvement from the flowering plants has also enhanced community engagement with the lake (Richter, 2024).



Reflection
What stands out about this solution is how it balances simplicity, affordability, and environmental impact. While the science behind these platforms is impressive, the real genius lies in their accessibility. By using locally available waste materials, the system becomes something that even resource-constrained communities can replicate. This highlights an important lesson in environmental innovation: solutions don&#x27;t always have to be high-tech to be effective.

However, the broader applicability of FTWS depends on more than just their technical merits. For this system to work long-term, it requires ongoing maintenance and strong community buy-in. If these platforms are neglected, they risk becoming just another source of pollution. But with proper education and engagement, FTWS could be a game-changer for polluted water bodies around the world, particularly in areas with limited budgets for environmental restoration.

I also find the dual benefit of these platforms intriguing—they not only clean the water but also improve the aesthetic and social value of lakes like Nagdaha. People are more likely to care for something that looks beautiful and benefits their community. This underscores how environmental solutions can have ripple effects beyond their immediate purpose, fostering a stronger connection between people and their natural surroundings.

In terms of challenges, the potential release of microplastics from the trash-based platforms is a valid concern. But compared to the sheer volume of pollutants they remove, this drawback seems manageable, especially if materials are selected thoughtfully. The success of this project shows that even small, incremental steps can lead to meaningful environmental change.

Source Attribution
Richter, H. (2024, July 25). Polluted lakes are being cleansed using floating wetlands made of trash. WIRED. Retrieved from https://www.wired.com/story/planet-pioneers-nagdaha-small-earth-nepal-soni-pradhanang-ftws-floating-treatment-wetland-systems-water-cleaning-pollution

Soldier Flies
Contains unread posts
Brook Caban posted Dec 4, 2024 9:30 PMLast edited: Thursday, December 5, 2024 10:49 AM EST
Hello Class, 

My NWR did not have much on its waste management so I chose to go with the second option. This weeks readings were interesting to me. I find with majority of the environmental science classes I have taken so far they discuss a lot about pollution and waste but they don&#x27;t necessarily go into specific detail on the processes used to clean up the damage that had occurred. They usually just say that it was taken care of or is still under monitoring which is what my NWR said.

A cool waste management practice that I found is using flies to break down waste. The specific fly I learned about is the soldier fly. They can take organic waste and convert it into biomass. It works by lewering the flies into a waste land with organic waste. Once the fly lays its eggs the larvae goes to town on the waste in the area. The super cool part about this is that they love to eat so they cover large amounts of waste at once. This method reduces green house gas emissions and is a natural way to combat food waste. The other amazing thing about this method is that the biomass created by the flies is high in nutrients and can be used for animal feed so instead of using soy based products farmers could be using the biomass produced instead. it is a creative and sustainable way to manage waste.  

https://youtu.be/uuxh7Yb308g?si=l3XDp9rZh1PPpqXv

This is a super good video to explain more on the process!

Beta Bugs Understanding the Life Cycle of the Black Soldier Fly ...

I personally believe this process is amazing. Its interesting to me that when we are at a bbq and flies come to our food we shoo them away but now there are processes in place that flies are to eat our left overs, poop it out, and we feed its waste to animals that we then turn into food again to eat...kind of gross if you ask me but still different none the less. It is not necessarily a process that helps everything as the flies are attracted to the organic waste so anything other than that this method wont be able to help with too much but I believe this is a wonderful approach and start to combatting organic waste while not creating more pollution in the clean up process. 

Brook

Bee Brick
Contains unread posts
Carlee Shaw posted Dec 4, 2024 11:12 AMLast edited: Thursday, December 5, 2024 10:48 AM EST
Hey Class!

Summary of the Story

The Bee Brick is an innovative construction material that doubles as a habitat for solitary bees. Designed by the UK-based company Green&amp;Blue, the brick features small holes where bees can nest and lay eggs. It&#x27;s made from upcycled waste material from the construction industry, transforming discarded concrete into an eco-friendly resource. The Bee Brick integrates seamlessly into urban and rural buildings, offering a creative way to address pollinator decline while reducing construction waste.

Why It&#x27;s Unusual

This approach stands out because it solves two distinct environmental issues—pollinator habitat loss and construction waste—using a single, scalable solution. While most efforts to support pollinators focus on gardens or natural areas, the Bee Brick brings this effort into the built environment, making it feasible for cities to support biodiversity.

Here is a short video showcasing the Bee Brick and its impact

The Bee Brick is an innovative solution with great potential, especially in urban areas where traditional pollinator habitats are scarce. Its scalability and simplicity make it applicable in various settings, including residential, commercial, and public buildings. However, its effectiveness is limited to solitary bees and might not address the needs of other pollinators like honeybees or butterflies. Additionally, widespread adoption requires education and buy-in from the construction industry and consumers.

Green&amp;Blue. (n.d.). The Bee Brick. Retrieved from https://www.greenandblue.co.uk

Smith, J. (2023). Urban biodiversity: Building for pollinators. Ecological Innovations Journal, 15(2), 56-62.

Carlee

Canaan Valley - Mazik
Contains unread posts
Joseph Mazik posted Dec 3, 2024 10:29 AM
Canaan Valley National Wildlife Refuge has a strong commitment to waste management, focused on protecting the ecosystems of the valley. The CCP highlights several types of waste they manage, including organic debris from natural processes, recreational waste left by visitors, and agricultural runoff from surrounding areas (p.123). These issues come from a combination of human activity, nearby land use, and natural factors.

To address these challenges, the refuge has applied a range of remedies. Public outreach plays a major role, with staff encouraging visitors to follow “pack-in, pack-out” policies to minimize litter. Regular cleanup events, often involving volunteers, help remove debris from trails and waterways (p. 89). Wetland buffer restoration is another key strategy, where native plants are used to filter agricultural runoff and stabilize soil, improving water quality and protecting habitats. Additionally, recycling stations placed around the refuge make it easier for visitors to dispose of waste responsibly (p.135).

Overall, I think the refuge is doing a solid job with their waste management practices. The combination of education, restoration, and clean-up efforts reflects a practical approach to keeping the refuge clean and sustainable. However, there’s always room for improvement. Adding advanced waste separation systems could help recycling efforts, and expanding partnerships with local farms could help reduce agricultural runoff even further. It would also be beneficial to invest in technology for real-time waste monitoring and public reporting to engage the community more actively in waste management efforts. In my opinion, the NWR’s current strategies are effective, but innovation will be key to success. The combination of public education, practical management, and community involvement is a strong foundation.

U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. (2011). Comprehensive Conservation Plan for Canaan Valley National Wildlife Refuge. Retrieved from U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service Website.

Portland turning Poop to Power
Contains unread posts
Kelly Orndorff posted Dec 3, 2024 4:15 PMLast edited: Tuesday, December 3, 2024 6:05 PM EST
This was such an enjoyable topic for me to find because I am always excited to read about new ways to help solve the pollution crisis. I have been passionate about keeping the planet clean and reusing resources since I was little; I would make sure to recycle every paper scrap I could when doing art projects and collect aluminum cans to bring to recycle for a few bucks.

In 2017 Portland, Oregon developed creative solutions to turn human waste into energy through an approach called “Poop to Power Project”. This strategy is an example of how societies can tackle waste management and produce renewable energy at the same time. When food or waste breaks decompose it creates methane, carbon dioxide and other gases. With this project wastewater will be collected and put into digesters that will not have oxygen in them (Newman, n.d.). This speeds up the process of breaking down the waste and creates biogas that is able to be collected and filtered then is available as natural gas. Portland is going to be able to contain and clean almost 100 percent of the biogas it produces in this manner and send it into a natural gas companies’ pipeline (Newman, n.d.). According to the city, they are also going to sell it as an alternative to diesel fuel and eventually power city trucks. Estimated impacts of the project annually are a reduction of 21,000 tons of greenhouse gas emissions, around 3 million dollars in revenue for the city, and replacing 1.34 million gallons of diesel (Newman, n.d.). The best part is knowing the resources will always be available.




Photo: Newman,n.d

I feel like this is an investment that every city should be looking into doing. Utilizing a potent greenhouse gas for renewable energy helps the climate change crisis. Poop to Power also encourages a circular economy and sustainable resource management. The facility will generate enough biogas to power city operations reducing the dependance on fossil fuels or external energy sources. Not only will this reduce the emissions from the city, but from keeping the energy where it is needed outside sources like trucks bringing in fuel, or disrupting the ecosystems to find natural gas is prevented. It creates a positive ripple effect in pollution, climate, and ecosystem conservation. This type of waste will NEVER go away, we might as well do something with it.

Source:

Newman, J. (n.d.). Poop to power: Turning Wastewater into Clean Energy. Portland.gov. https://www.portland.gov/bes/resource-recovery/poop-power

Advanced Recycling
Contains unread posts
Kimberly Wolf posted Dec 4, 2024 11:17 PMLast edited: Thursday, December 5, 2024 10:49 AM EST
Presently there is no solid waste collection and disposal on refuge lands. 

Instead, I chose option 2 and wrote about advanced recycling waste. I read about it through Exxon mobile. It is always shocking and also not surprising to me when I read about how much plastic there is on the world. According to the UN Environment Program we produce about 400 million tonnes of plastic waste globally each year. With global production expecting to reach 1,100 million tonnes by 2050 (UNEP, n.d). 

Advanced Recycling is working to improve recycling rates. At this point only about 9 percent of plastic is recycled. This is because it is very difficult to remove oils, grease and food waste from used plastics, as well as, materials that are multilayers. Mechanical recycling cannot effectively separate them. Advanced recycling is about to break down material to its molecular level. The molecules are then used to create fuels, lubricates and new plastics. By recovering a greater percentage of plastics from the landfills it is able to create a more circular economy. This system is also able to be created and used around the world. 

Advanced recycling is transforming the way plastics are sorted and processed. It allows for the diversion of difficult-to-recycle materials like chip bags and artificial turf to plastic recovery facilities. These facilities convert the plastics into liquid and gas molecules, which are then used to create valuable products. The Baytown, Texas facility currently has 40,000 metric tons of annual processing capacity, with plans to build 500,000 metric tons globally by 2027. The amount of usable raw materials processed from plastic waste is attributed through a mass balance approach to the &quot;certified-circular plastics.&quot;



I had no idea they were able to do this with plastics so I am hoping they are able to expand on this and take care of our planet. 


Kimberly Wolf

The Ocean Cleanup
Contains unread posts
Kyle Bruce posted Dec 4, 2024 11:51 AMLast edited: Thursday, December 5, 2024 10:48 AM EST
The Ocean Cleanup is an innovative project aimed at removing plastic pollution from the world&#x27;s oceans. Founded by Dutch inventor Boyan Slat, this initiative uses large-scale systems to capture plastic debris in the ocean&#x27;s gyres, areas where ocean currents concentrate waste. The organization&#x27;s primary focus is on the Great Pacific Garbage Patch, one of the largest accumulations of marine debris in the world.

The cleanup system consists of a floating barrier that captures plastic using the ocean&#x27;s natural currents. This passive approach avoids harming marine life and relies on solar-powered systems for efficiency. Once collected, the debris is brought to shore for sorting and recycling. Since 2018, The Ocean Cleanup has successfully removed thousands of tons of plastic and is continually improving its technology to enhance effectiveness.

Ocean Cleanup

How It Works
The System: The cleanup system includes a U-shaped barrier that concentrates plastic waste as it drifts with the ocean&#x27;s currents. A skirt extends below the surface to catch plastics while allowing marine life to pass underneath.
Collection: Plastic waste is funneled into a retention zone at the center of the system. Periodically, the waste is removed by vessels and transported to shore for recycling.
Sustainability: The project ensures minimal environmental impact by using solar power and wind energy to run the systems.
Reflection
The Ocean Cleanup is a groundbreaking approach to tackling marine pollution. Its passive design, which uses natural ocean currents, makes it both energy-efficient and eco-friendly. However, its effectiveness is largely limited to ocean gyres where plastic accumulates and does not address pollution sources like rivers or coastal areas as directly.

Despite these limitations, it&#x27;s a scalable solution that could drastically reduce the amount of plastic in our oceans if paired with broader waste prevention efforts on land. This project is a game-changer, but it&#x27;s only part of the solution; we also need global policies to reduce plastic production and consumption.

Video

References:
Slat, B. (2018). The Great Pacific Garbage Patch. Retrieved from https://www.theoceancleanup.com

The Ocean Cleanup. (2018, October 4). The Great Pacific Garbage Patch - The Ocean Cleanup. YouTube. https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=O1EAeNdTFHU

W5: WasteShark
Contains unread posts
Nicholas Schutz posted Dec 7, 2024 2:29 AM
Everyone,

Upon initial research into my NWR, the Iroquois National Wildlife Refuge, I realize that there is not an extensive plan for waste management as many of us have found this week. I found a section in the Comprehensive Conservation Plan (CCP) for my Refuge that addresses littering and dumping of waste. It mostly covers guidelines that I would interpret as being pack-in-pack-out as to not leave trash behind. That means there wouldn’t be any trash cans or dumpsters within the refuge leading to hopefully deter people from just leaving waste behind. This lessens the need to have a waste management plan. With that being said I decided to tackle the second option this week.

Advancements in modern technology have proven detrimental to combating waste. One advancement to highlight is the WasteShark that has been working to clean up our ocean ports and harbors. A completely autonomous and electric aquadrone helps to collect plastic debris floating on the water’s surface. One drone can collect about 48 gallons of trash in its basket as it propels forward in the water with a gate mechanism that closes to trap the trash inside if the drone needs to change direction and move in reverse. In one period of operation which lasts from 6 to 10 hours, one Shark can collect 350kgs or 771.62lbs of waste that is polluting a waterway. Being that the WasteShark is meant to operate in smaller waterways such as canals and harbors, LiDAR is equipped onto the drone to provide it with “eyes” which keep it situationally aware to avoid collisions with ships or other boats operating in the area. This drone comes with many notable advantages when it comes to the topic of waterway cleanup. There are different types of WasteSharks meant to do different jobs in different environments. There are some that can be manually controlled or completely autonomous (some have both options). They produce zero carbon emissions by operating on electricity. They have been advertised as being simple to operate and easy to store and transport. This drone’s design was developed off the anatomy of a whale shark (hence the name) which has a very large and wide mouth. Technological engineering made simple, sustainable, and forward-thinking in an era that needs more advancements to combat waste issues. These are the projects we love to read about and see implemented. 





Resources:

Solutions, D. (n.d.). Drone solutions. Drone Solutions. https://dronesolutionservices.com/wasteshark

WasteShark. (n.d.). Wevolver. https://www.wevolver.com/specs/wasteshark

The Interceptor: cleaning rivers
Contains unread posts
Sierra Barker posted Dec 4, 2024 4:21 PM
Smmary
The Ocean Cleanup’s Interceptor is a device that helps clean up rivers by collecting trash before it makes it into the ocean. Scientists realized that rivers are one of the main ways plastic pollution gets into the ocean, so they designed the Interceptor to stop it at the source.

The Interceptor looks like a sleek, futuristic boat and works by funneling trash onto a conveyor belt. The trash then gets lifted onto the boat and sorted into dumpsters. Once the dumpsters are full, they’re taken to shore for recycling or disposal. It runs on solar power, so it’s eco-friendly, and it can work on its own with minium human supervision. Since it started in 2019, Interceptors have been placed in several rivers, including ones in Indonesia and the Dominican Republic, where they’ve collected thousands of pounds of trash.




Reflection
I think the Interceptor is an awesome idea because it stops pollution before it even gets to the ocean. It’s also powered by solar energy, which makes it sustainable, and it can collect a ton of trash every day. However, it seems like it would work best in rivers with a lot of trash floating on the surface. In smaller rivers or places where trash isn’t as visible, it might not be as effective.

Another challenge is that once the trash is collected, there has to be a good recycling or waste disposal system nearby, or it could create new problems. Still, I think this is a really innovative solution, and it could be used in a lot of rivers around the world to help reduce plastic pollution. It’s definitely a step in the right direction for cleaner waterways and healthier oceans!

                                                                           References 

The Ocean Cleanup. (n.d.). Interceptor original. The Ocean Cleanup. Retrieved December 4, 2024, from https://theoceancleanup.com/rivers/interceptor-original/The Ocean Cleanup. (n.d.). Kingston, Jamaica: The first

Interceptor city. The Ocean Cleanup. Retrieved December 4, 2024, from  https://theoceancleanup.com/updates/kingston-jamaica-the-first-interceptor-city/

White River NRW - Miller
Contains unread posts
Trevor Miller posted Dec 1, 2024 1:18 PM
Hello everyone,

This class if flying by in my opinion. Before we know it, it will be over. This week I&#x27;m choosing to add to my final project of Dale Bumpers White River NWR in Arkansas.

db

cans

Waste Management Summary
The Dale Bumpers White River National Wildlife Refuge (NWR) in Arkansas primarily addresses waste management through prevention and mitigation measures outlined in its Comprehensive Conservation Plan (CCP). The refuge focuses on maintaining ecosystem health by tackling several waste-related concerns:

Types of Waste:

Solid Waste: From recreational activities like hunting, fishing, and camping, as well as maintenance operations. Examples include litter and equipment debris.
Hazardous Waste: Potential contamination from nearby agricultural runoff, chemical spills, or unauthorized dumping of harmful substances.
Biological Waste: Waste generated from managing invasive species or vegetation clearance.
Causes of Waste:

Visitor activities contribute to solid waste.
Agricultural and industrial runoff from surrounding areas introduces chemical pollutants.
Management operations, including habitat restoration and invasive species control, produce biological waste.
Remedies and Management Actions:

Regular maintenance and cleanup efforts, including volunteer-led initiatives.
Educational programs to promote responsible use of refuge resources.
Partnerships with local agencies to monitor and mitigate external pollution sources.
Habitat restoration projects that naturally reduce the need for heavy chemical or mechanical interventions.
Adequacy of Efforts
The refuge is proactive in addressing waste issues, particularly through community involvement and preventive measures. These strategies align with the refuge&#x27;s ecological priorities, minimizing long-term harm.

Suggestions for Improvement
While current actions are commendable, there is room for enhanced efforts:

Waste Tracking Systems: Implementing a digital tracking system for waste sources could help address persistent issues more efficiently.
Green Infrastructure: Developing more eco-friendly infrastructure, like biodegradable facilities, would reduce the overall waste footprint.
Stronger Collaboration: Partnering more extensively with local businesses and governments could ensure external pollution sources are minimized.
These additions could bolster the refuge&#x27;s ability to maintain its ecological integrity while accommodating public use.

I&#x27;m really looking forward to wrapping up this final project. I&#x27;ve learned an incredible amount of information about a NWR that&#x27;s right down the street from me. 

-TA

References:

-Dale Bumpers White River NWR CCP, U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. retrieved from https://www.fws.gov/refuge/dale-bumpers-white-river date accessed Nov 17, 2024

Aspergillus tubingensis
Contains unread posts
Zachary Minor posted Dec 3, 2024 10:07 PMLast edited: Thursday, December 5, 2024 10:51 AM EST
Hello class,

      I have chosen option to I thought it was a different but cool discussion to write about. 


     One of the most unusual approaches to waste prevention and cleanup involves the use of fungi specific Aspergillus tubingensis, to break down plastic waste. Discovered in a landfill in Pakistan this fungus can degrade plastics like polyurethane in a matter of weeks. it does so by treating enzymes that break the chemical bonds and plastic, turning it into simpler organic compounds. This process happens significantly faster than the hundred of years it typically takes for plastic with the grade naturally. The story of Aspergillus tubingensis Has sparked interest in the potential for fungi to play a significant role in solving the global plastic pollution crisis. Several research institutions are now exploring a scale this process, aim to deploy fungi in landfills or develop industrial reactors that use fungi to treat plastic waste efficiently. 


     Wow this message shows great promise, it may not yet be widely applicable. its effectiveness is highly dependent on controlled environments, such as maintaining the correct temperature, moisture levels, and pH. For now, this limit is used to specific cases where those conditions can be regulated, such as an industrial treatment facilities. However, with further research in development, fungi base plastic degradation could become a groundbreaking solution applicable on a global scale. 


     This method is particularly exciting because it represents a shift towards biological solution to human made problems, leveraging natures processes to address environmental issues. it also aligned with the circular economy principles, aiming to reduce waste and promote sustainable resource management. 
https://youtu.be/_OzvieulJc4?si=zwhay9RChn-kiR3W



sources-

---

**ASSISTANT**:
The Billy Frank Jr. Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge, established in 1974, is dedicated to preserving the Nisqually River Delta&#x27;s diverse ecosystems, which support over 250 species of birds and various other wildlife. While the refuge&#x27;s primary focus is on habitat conservation and restoration, particularly the significant estuary restoration in 2009, its Comprehensive Conservation Plan (CCP) does not explicitly detail waste management practices.

**Types and Causes of Waste:**

- **Visitor-Generated Waste:** With activities like bird watching, hiking, and educational tours, visitors may inadvertently leave behind litter, including food wrappers, plastic bottles, and other disposables.

- **Operational Waste:** Maintenance and habitat restoration activities can produce waste materials such as construction debris, plant trimmings, and equipment-related waste.

**Current Waste Management Practices:**

- **&quot;Pack-In, Pack-Out&quot; Policy:** The refuge encourages visitors to carry out all trash they bring in, minimizing on-site waste accumulation.

- **Recycling Initiatives:** Placement of recycling stations at strategic locations to facilitate proper disposal of recyclable materials.

- **Volunteer Cleanup Programs:** Organizing regular events where volunteers assist in collecting litter and maintaining the cleanliness of trails and waterways.

**Reflection on Waste Management Effectiveness:**

The refuge&#x27;s current strategies demonstrate a commitment to maintaining a pristine environment. The &quot;pack-in, pack-out&quot; policy fosters visitor responsibility, and volunteer programs enhance community engagement. However, the absence of detailed waste management protocols in the CCP suggests room for improvement.

**Recommendations for Improvement:**

1. **Comprehensive Waste Management Plan:** Developing a detailed plan within the CCP to address waste management systematically.

2. **Educational Outreach:** Implementing programs to educate visitors on the impacts of littering and the importance of waste reduction.

3. **Enhanced Recycling Facilities:** Increasing the number and visibility of recycling stations to encourage proper waste segregation.

4. **Monitoring and Evaluation:** Establishing systems to monitor waste generation and disposal patterns to inform future strategies.

By integrating these measures, the refuge can strengthen its conservation efforts and ensure a cleaner environment for both wildlife and visitors.

**References:**

U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. (2004). *Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan and Environmental Impact Statement*. Retrieved from https://www.fws.gov/refuge/billy-frank-jr-nisqually

Pacific Northwest National Laboratory. (n.d.). *SSM Habitat Restoration: Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge*. Retrieved from https://www.pnnl.gov/ssm-habitat-restoration-nisqually-national-wildlife-refuge

Thurston County, where the Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR) is located, has a significant proportion of privately owned lands, particularly south of the Nisqually wetland area. The county has initiated robust reforestation projects near these wetlands, which play a crucial role in environmental conservation.

**Impact of Reforestation on Pollution Reduction and Soil Stability**

Reforestation efforts in Thurston County contribute to pollution reduction and soil stabilization in several ways:

- **Pollution Reduction:** Trees act as natural air filters by absorbing pollutants such as carbon monoxide, sulfur dioxide, and ozone. They also sequester carbon dioxide, mitigating climate change impacts. citeturn0search24

- **Soil Stability:** Tree roots bind soil particles together, reducing erosion caused by wind and water. This stabilization prevents landslides and maintains soil health, which is vital for local agriculture and water quality.

**Reforestation Projects in Thurston County**

The county&#x27;s commitment to reforestation is evident through initiatives like the Healthy Forests Project, which aims to update forest conversion ordinances and review tree protection standards. citeturn0search9 Additionally, the Thurston Conservation District&#x27;s strategic plans emphasize sustainable resource management and conservation efforts. citeturn0search11

**Integration of Agroforestry**

Agroforestry, the integration of trees and shrubs into agricultural landscapes, offers multiple benefits:

- **Biodiversity Enhancement:** Incorporating diverse plant species creates habitats for various wildlife, promoting ecological balance.

- **Economic Viability:** Diversified production systems can provide additional income streams for landowners, enhancing economic resilience.

Considering your interest in building highly biodiverse Miyawaki forests, agroforestry practices can be tailored to create dense, native forests that restore ecosystems and provide economic benefits.

**Nisqually NWR (Steward)**

**Thurston County’s Reforestation Potential and Nisqually NWR’s Role**  
Located within Thurston County, the Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR) benefits significantly from the region’s private landownership and proximity to strong reforestation projects. Thurston County offers a unique opportunity to restore up to 108,000 acres of forest cover, with an estimated CO₂ sequestration potential of 691,000 tons per year. This initiative aligns with broader efforts to combat pollution and stabilize soils in critical areas adjacent to the Nisqually wetland ecosystem.

**How Reforestation Enhances Pollution Mitigation and Soil Stability**  
The Reforestation Hub data shows that Thurston County’s greatest reforestation potential lies in pasturelands (51,000 acres) and grassy areas (10,600 acres). These projects can act as natural barriers, reducing agricultural runoff and protecting the wetland’s hydrology. Reforestation enhances soil stability, especially in areas south of Nisqually, where degraded lands are prone to erosion. The increase in tree cover also filters pollutants before they enter streams, reinforcing the wetland&#x27;s resilience.

**Nisqually NWR and the Role of Reforestation in Conservation**  
The Nisqually CCP emphasizes the refuge’s role in preserving wetland ecosystems and supporting species diversity. Adjacent reforestation projects amplify these efforts by providing buffer zones that combat external stressors such as pollution from urban and agricultural areas. This synergy ensures that wetland health is maintained, supporting migratory birds and aquatic life.

**Agroforestry as a Complementary Practice**  
As I deepen my professional journey toward forestry and ecosystem restoration, the principles of agroforestry offer a path to integrate sustainable land use with biodiversity goals. Agroforestry practices—such as integrating Miyawaki forests—can fast-track forest establishment while providing food, timber, and other benefits to local communities. This aligns with my personal mission to create highly biodiverse forests that restore ecosystems and mitigate climate change.

**A Reflection on the Broader Impact**  
Reforestation in Thurston County is not just about planting trees; it’s about leveraging nature’s ability to heal itself. By coupling reforestation projects with robust conservation plans like the Nisqually CCP, we can create systems where wetlands and forests coexist symbiotically. This reaffirms my belief that restoration efforts are most effective when they center on the interconnectedness of ecosystems.

Nisqually NWR (Steward)
Hey everyone, sorry for jumping in a bit late here. Life’s been a bit chaotic, but I’m excited to share what I’ve learned so far about pollution issues at Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR). This place is pretty amazing. It is located down at the southern end of Puget Sound in Washington, near Olympia, and it’s vital for many migratory birds and marine species. Unfortunately, this beautiful area isn’t immune to pollution problems.
Types and Causes of Pollution:
So, one big issue is water pollution. Urban and agricultural runoff carries a whole mix of nutrients, pesticides, and other pollutants into the estuarine waters. The extra nutrients can trigger these gnarly algal blooms, which then deplete oxygen levels and endanger aquatic species. Another problem is sediment runoff, especially when storms hit, eroding land along the Nisqually River and making water quality worse.
Another thing that’s on the radar is marine debris, mainly plastics and trash from Puget Sound. Wildlife gets tangled up in it or accidentally eats it, which is rough. The problem is made worse by people visiting the area and littering, plus the waste management in surrounding towns not being up to par.
And then there’s air pollution—it’s not as talked about, but emissions from vehicles and industrial activities nearby can settle onto the land and water, affecting plant life and the overall health of the habitat.
Management and Remedies:
Nisqually’s team has a lot going on to fight these issues. One of the coolest things is the 2009 estuarine restoration project, where they reconnected almost 700 acres of land to tidal flow. This was huge for improving water quality and habitat resilience, and it helps filter out pollutants. For runoff, they’ve been creating buffer zones along the Nisqually River to catch sediment before it gets into the water. Plus, they’re working with local farmers to reduce nutrient runoff using best practices.
They also have good public education programs to teach visitors about proper waste management and why it matters.
My Thoughts on Their Efforts:
I think they’re doing a solid job, especially with the massive restoration project. It’s pretty impressive. But there’s always more that could be done. Partnering more with local cities and towns to tackle upstream pollution would probably greatly help. Maybe they could even push for more green infrastructure projects to reduce runoff. And with plastic pollution, more frequent community clean-up events or stricter local regulations could be a game-changer.
Managing an area like Nisqually is about balance using a multi-faceted approach. The refuge is a critical piece of the Puget Sound ecosystem, and keeping it clean is a big deal not just for wildlife but for the whole region’s health.
I would love to hear any ideas you have on how these kinds of strategies could work elsewhere, or if you have any extra thoughts on what else they could do!
References:
U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. (n.d.). Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge: Comprehensive Conservation Plan. Retrieved from https://www.fws.gov/refuge/nisqually 
Puget Sound Partnership. (2024). Addressing Urban and Agricultural Runoff. Retrieved from https://www.psp.wa.gov 
Here’s a map of the estuarine restoration area—it&#x27;s wild how much land was reconnected:

[SOURCE} https://www.nisquallydeltarestoration.org/science_estuary_nearshore_changes.php 
And a quick video about keeping Puget Sound waters clean:

---

**Nisqually NWR (Steward)**  

Hey everyone, I’m coming in a little late to the conversation, but I wanted to make sure to share something worthwhile about waste management at the Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR). As you might know, this refuge is located in Thurston County, at the southern end of Puget Sound. It’s an incredible place for biodiversity, especially as a critical stopover for migratory birds. That said, waste management is an ongoing challenge for protecting this unique ecosystem.  

**Waste Management Challenges and Causes**  
The Nisqually NWR faces several waste-related challenges that can impact its delicate balance. The biggest issues are litter and agricultural runoff. Visitor activity is a major contributor to litter—everything from wrappers to fishing lines left behind on trails and shorelines. Even with pack-in, pack-out policies, some waste inevitably lingers. Agricultural runoff from surrounding lands adds another layer of complexity. It brings sediment, nutrients, and pesticides into the wetlands, threatening water quality.  

Another overlooked issue is waste from nearby urban areas. Thurston County has a high percentage of private land ownership, and while this supports development and farming, it also increases the potential for unmanaged waste to enter natural areas.  

**Management Practices and Their Impact**  
The Nisqually NWR team has been proactive in addressing these challenges. Their most notable work is in public education—signage and outreach programs encourage visitors to leave no trace. They’ve also collaborated with local farmers to adopt practices like vegetative buffers, which help trap sediment and prevent it from entering the refuge’s waterways.  

A particularly inspiring part of their work is the focus on restoration. Projects like the estuarine restoration in 2009 not only reconnected 700 acres of tidal wetlands but also created natural waste filtration systems. These wetlands act as “living filters,” capturing sediments and improving water quality.  

Reforestation efforts near the wetlands, particularly in Thurston County, are another critical layer of protection. The county is working on restoring grassy areas and pastures south of Nisqually. These projects stabilize the soil, reduce runoff, and provide additional carbon sequestration, which ties directly into pollution reduction.  

**Looking at Opportunities for Improvement**  
While Nisqually’s waste management practices are strong, there’s always room to level up. For example, partnering more closely with local governments to improve urban waste management upstream could prevent trash from entering the refuge. Reforestation projects near urban areas—like those highlighted by the Reforestation Hub—could also act as natural barriers to pollution. Thurston County alone has 108,000 acres of potential reforestation, which could remove 691,000 tons of CO₂ annually while improving soil health.  

Agroforestry could also play a role. Imagine integrating Miyawaki forests—small, dense, biodiverse forests—around agricultural lands near Nisqually. This approach could not only boost biodiversity but also trap sediment and filter water before it reaches the wetlands. Agroforestry’s potential to merge conservation with productivity feels like a win-win for this region.  

**Final Thoughts**  
Waste management at Nisqually NWR is about more than picking up trash; it’s about creating systems that naturally prevent waste from ever becoming a problem. Projects like the estuarine restoration and reforestation nearby are essential for keeping this refuge thriving. As someone increasingly drawn to forestry and ecosystem restoration, I’m inspired by how these strategies align with larger conservation goals.  

If you’ve got ideas on how to bring agroforestry or reforestation into other waste management plans, I’d love to hear them!  

---

**References**  
American Forests. (n.d.). *Reforestation Hub*. Retrieved December 7, 2024, from [https://reforestationhub.org](https://reforestationhub.org)  

U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. (2005). *Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan*. Retrieved from [CCP link](https://www.nisquallydeltarestoration.org/pdf/US%20Fish%20and%20Wildlife%20Service%202005%20Nisqually%20National%20Wildlife%20Refuge%20Final%20Comprehensive%20Conservation%20Plan%20304p.pdf)  

Reforestation Hub. (2024). *Thurston County Reforestation Opportunities*. Retrieved from [https://reforestationhub.org](https://reforestationhub.org)  

What do you think? Should we add a map or a visual of Thurston County’s reforestation potential to emphasize these points? Let me know how else we can tweak this!

**ASSISTANT**:
### Nisqually NWR (Steward)

Hey everyone, I’m coming in a bit late again, but I’ve been diving deep into what makes the Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR) such a critical space, not only for wildlife but for its broader environmental significance. The topic of waste and pollution management is closely tied to its surrounding ecosystem, and as we peel back the layers, it becomes clear how connected everything is—land, water, air, and, most importantly, people. Let’s explore.

---

**Waste Management Practices at Nisqually NWR**

The Nisqually NWR is nestled where the Nisqually River meets Puget Sound. While the Comprehensive Conservation Plan (CCP) doesn’t focus heavily on &quot;waste management&quot; in a conventional sense, it tackles issues of pollution and restoration that directly affect waste pathways.

The refuge faces challenges like sediment runoff from agricultural and urban areas, marine debris washing into the estuary, and nutrient loading from surrounding farms. Sediments and excess nutrients fuel algal blooms that deplete oxygen levels and threaten aquatic species. On the human side, littering by visitors and inadequate waste management upstream also make their way into the wetland systems. 

---

**Remedies and Solutions**

Nisqually NWR has implemented a number of strategies to address these interconnected problems. One of the biggest game-changers was the 2009 estuarine restoration project, which restored tidal flow to nearly 700 acres. This not only improved water quality but also acted as a natural filter to capture pollutants.

For sediment runoff, the refuge collaborates with local farms to establish buffer zones and implement sustainable agricultural practices. These buffers also work as biodiversity corridors, connecting habitats. Community outreach programs encourage visitors to follow “pack-in, pack-out” waste policies and actively participate in cleanup events.

But what really excites me is how reforestation efforts, particularly in Thurston County, overlap with the refuge’s mission. Thurston County has one of the highest levels of private land ownership in the state, and much of this land south of Nisqually is ripe for reforestation. According to the *Reforestation Hub*, the county could restore 108,000 acres, potentially sequestering 691,000 tons of CO₂ annually. These reforestation projects stabilize soil, reduce erosion, and act as natural pollution sinks, complementing the refuge’s efforts.

---

**My Reflections**

Nisqually is doing incredible work, and its ecological and community-centered approach is inspiring. However, I see opportunities to tie in agroforestry practices as an additional layer of sustainability. Imagine integrating Miyawaki forests—dense, native, biodiverse tree clusters—into Thurston County&#x27;s reforestation projects to amplify the region’s ability to absorb pollutants and stabilize ecosystems. Agroforestry could also be a viable method for private landowners, blending productivity with conservation.

I’m especially drawn to this topic because much of my professional interest revolves around reforestation and afforestation. Projects like these align perfectly with my long-term goals of building biodiversity-focused forests and contributing to climate resilience. For me, Nisqually NWR isn’t just a refuge; it’s a blueprint for how we can rewild, restore, and reconnect ecosystems.

---

**Closing Thoughts**

The Nisqually NWR exemplifies how waste management isn’t just about trash cans and recycling—it’s about systems thinking. It’s about addressing upstream challenges, working with the land to heal itself, and fostering collaboration between communities and nature. Whether through buffers, tidal restoration, or even the potential for reforestation, this refuge shows what’s possible when we prioritize harmony over exploitation. 

What do you think? Could reforestation and agroforestry play an even bigger role in addressing pollution and waste? Let’s discuss!

---

**References**

**ASSISTANT**:
### Nisqually NWR (Steward)

Hey everyone, I’m coming in a bit late again, but I’ve been diving deep into what makes the Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR) such a vital space—not just for wildlife but for its broader environmental significance. The topic of waste and pollution management is closely tied to its surrounding ecosystem. As we peel back the layers, it becomes clear how connected everything is—land, water, air, and, most importantly, people. Let’s dive in.

---

**Waste Management Practices at Nisqually NWR**

The Nisqually NWR sits at the confluence of the Nisqually River and Puget Sound, a dynamic meeting place of land and water. While the Comprehensive Conservation Plan (CCP) doesn’t emphasize &quot;waste management&quot; in the traditional sense, it addresses pollution and restoration issues that directly shape waste pathways.

The refuge faces challenges like sediment runoff from agricultural and urban areas, marine debris washing into the estuary, and nutrient loading from nearby farms. Excess sediments and nutrients create conditions ripe for algal blooms, depleting oxygen levels and threatening aquatic species. On the human side, litter from visitors and inadequate waste management upstream also contribute to pollution in this sensitive ecosystem.

---

**Remedies and Solutions**

The Nisqually NWR has implemented several strategies to address these interconnected issues. One of the most transformative projects was the 2009 estuarine restoration initiative, which restored tidal flow to nearly 700 acres of wetlands. This move not only enhanced water quality but also created natural filtration systems that trap pollutants.

To combat sediment runoff, the refuge collaborates with local farmers to establish buffer zones and adopt sustainable agricultural practices. These buffer zones also serve as biodiversity corridors, helping to link fragmented habitats. Community outreach efforts encourage visitors to practice “pack-in, pack-out” waste management and participate in cleanup events, fostering a shared sense of stewardship.

What excites me most is the overlap between Nisqually’s mission and reforestation efforts in Thurston County. With one of the highest levels of private land ownership in Washington, Thurston County offers immense potential for reforestation just south of the refuge. According to the *Reforestation Hub*, 108,000 acres in Thurston County could be restored, potentially sequestering 691,000 tons of CO₂ annually. These projects would stabilize soil, reduce erosion, and act as pollution sinks, aligning beautifully with the refuge’s goals.

---

**My Reflections**

Nisqually NWR’s efforts are inspiring, particularly its ecological and community-centered approach. However, I see a unique opportunity to integrate agroforestry practices for added sustainability. Picture Miyawaki forests—dense, biodiverse, and native tree clusters—woven into Thurston County’s reforestation projects. Such forests could accelerate carbon sequestration, improve soil stability, and create microhabitats for wildlife. Agroforestry could also provide a win-win for private landowners, blending conservation with productivity.

This topic resonates with me on a personal and professional level. Much of my work revolves around reforestation and afforestation. Projects like these directly support my long-term vision of creating biodiversity-rich forests and contributing to climate resilience. For me, Nisqually NWR isn’t just a case study for this course—it’s a blueprint for how we can rewild, restore, and reconnect ecosystems. These principles are at the heart of the business model I’m actively refining alongside my studies.

---

**Closing Thoughts**

The Nisqually NWR demonstrates that waste management isn’t just about bins and recycling; it’s about systems thinking. It’s about addressing upstream challenges, partnering with the land to heal itself, and fostering collaboration between communities and nature. Whether through buffer zones, tidal restoration, or reforestation, the refuge showcases what’s possible when we prioritize harmony over exploitation.

What do you think? I find solutions rooted in first principles to be the most effective—and they’re far more motivating than simply dwelling on pollution. Let’s discuss!

---

**References**

American Forests. (n.d.). *Reforestation Hub*. Retrieved from https://reforestationhub.org  

U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. (2005). *Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge Final Comprehensive Conservation Plan*. Retrieved from https://www.nisquallydeltarestoration.org/pdf/US%20Fish%20and%20Wildlife%20Service%202005%20Nisqually%20National%20Wildlife%20Refuge%20Final%20Comprehensive%20Conservation%20Plan%20304p.pdf  

University of Missouri. (n.d.). *Master’s in Agroforestry*. Retrieved from https://online.missouri.edu

**Nisqually NWR (Steward)**  
Hey everyone, I’m coming in a bit late again. Still, I’ve been diving deep into what makes the Nisqually National Wildlife Refuge (NWR) such an important space for wildlife and its broader environmental significance. ### Waste Management Practices at Nisqually NWR  
The Nisqually NWR is nestled where the Nisqually River meets Puget Sound. While the Comprehensive Conservation Plan (CCP) doesn’t focus heavily on &quot;waste management&quot; in a conventional sense, it tackles issues of pollution and restoration that directly affect waste pathways.

The refuge faces challenges like sediment runoff from agricultural and urban areas, marine debris washing into the estuary, and nutrient loading from surrounding farms. Sediments and excess nutrients fuel algal blooms that deplete oxygen levels and threaten aquatic species. On the human side, littering by visitors and inadequate waste management upstream also make their way into the wetland systems.

### Remedies and Solutions

One of the biggest game-changers was the 2009 estuarine restoration project, which restored tidal flow to nearly 700 acres. This improved water quality and also acted as a natural filter to capture pollutants.  

For sediment runoff, the refuge collaborates with local farms to establish buffer zones and implement sustainable agricultural practices. These buffers also work as biodiversity corridors, connecting habitats. What really excites me is how reforestation efforts, particularly in Thurston County, overlap with the refuge’s mission. ### My Reflections

However, I see opportunities to tie in agroforestry practices as an additional layer of sustainability. Imagine integrating Miyawaki forests—dense, native, biodiverse tree clusters—into Thurston County&#x27;s reforestation projects to amplify the region’s ability to absorb pollutants and stabilize ecosystems.
